import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F

class InceptionModule(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_1x1, reduce_3x3, out_3x3, reduce_5x5, out_5x5, pool_proj):
        """
        :param in_channels: Number of input channels
        :param out_1x1: Number of output channels for the 1x1 convolution
        :param reduce_3x3: Number of output channels for the 1x1 reduction before 3x3 convolution
        :param out_3x3: Number of output channels for the 3x3 convolution
        :param reduce_5x5: Number of output channels for the 1x1 reduction before 5x5 convolution
        :param out_5x5: Number of output channels for the 5x5 convolution
        :param pool_proj: Number of output channels for the pooling projection
        """
        super(InceptionModule, self).__init__()
        
        # 1x1 convolution branch
        self.branch1x1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_1x1, kernel_size=1)
        
        # 3x3 convolution branch
        self.branch3x3 = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_3x3, kernel_size=1),
            nn.Conv2d(reduce_3x3, out_3x3, kernel_size=3, padding=1)
        )
        
        # 5x5 convolution branch
        self.branch5x5 = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_5x5, kernel_size=1),
            nn.Conv2d(reduce_5x5, out_5x5, kernel_size=5, padding=2)
        )
        
        # Max pooling branch
        self.branch_pool = nn.Sequential(
            nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1),
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, pool_proj, kernel_size=1)
        )
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, out_channels, height, width)
        """
        branch1x1 = self.branch1x1(x)
        branch3x3 = self.branch3x3(x)
        branch5x5 = self.branch5x5(x)
        branch_pool = self.branch_pool(x)
        
        outputs = [branch1x1, branch3x3, branch5x5, branch_pool]
        return torch.cat(outputs, 1)

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, num_classes=1000):
        """
        :param num_classes: Number of output classes
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(3, 64, kernel_size=7, stride=2, padding=3)
        self.maxpool1 = nn.MaxPool2d(3, stride=2, padding=1)
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(64, 64, kernel_size=1)
        self.conv3 = nn.Conv2d(64, 192, kernel_size=3, padding=1)
        self.maxpool2 = nn.MaxPool2d(3, stride=2, padding=1)
        
        self.inception3a = InceptionModule(192, 64, 96, 128, 16, 32, 32)
        self.inception3b = InceptionModule(256, 128, 128, 192, 32, 96, 64)
        self.maxpool3 = nn.MaxPool2d(3, stride=2, padding=1)
        
        self.inception4a = InceptionModule(480, 192, 96, 208, 16, 48, 64)
        self.inception4b = InceptionModule(512, 160, 112, 224, 24, 64, 64)
        self.inception4c = InceptionModule(512, 128, 128, 256, 24, 64, 64)
        self.inception4d = InceptionModule(512, 112, 144, 288, 32, 64, 64)
        self.inception4e = InceptionModule(528, 256, 160, 320, 32, 128, 128)
        self.maxpool4 = nn.MaxPool2d(3, stride=2, padding=1)
        
        self.inception5a = InceptionModule(832, 256, 160, 320, 32, 128, 128)
        self.inception5b = InceptionModule(832, 384, 192, 384, 48, 128, 128)
        
        self.avgpool = nn.AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1, 1))
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(0.0)
        self.fc = nn.Linear(1024, num_classes)
    
    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, 3, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, num_classes)
        """
        x = self.maxpool1(F.relu(self.conv1(x)))
        x = F.relu(self.conv2(x))
        x = self.maxpool2(F.relu(self.conv3(x)))
        
        x = self.inception3a(x)
        x = self.inception3b(x)
        x = self.maxpool3(x)
        
        x = self.inception4a(x)
        x = self.inception4b(x)
        x = self.inception4c(x)
        x = self.inception4d(x)
        x = self.inception4e(x)
        x = self.maxpool4(x)
        
        x = self.inception5a(x)
        x = self.inception5b(x)
        
        x = self.avgpool(x)
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)
        x = self.dropout(x)
        x = self.fc(x)
        
        return x

# Test code
batch_size = 10
input_channels = 3
height = 224
width = 224
num_classes = 1000

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, input_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [num_classes]

Fusing avoids the separate writes and the cat copy: we read input once (or once per branch but from cache), and write output once. This could save 2x memory bandwidth. But we can also write a generic kernel that uses shared memory for input tiles and register accumulation. Let's design a generic direct conv kernel. **Generic Direct Conv Kernel Design:** Input layout: (B, C_in, H, W) Weight layout: (C_out, C_in, K, K) Output layout: (B, C_out, H_out, W_out) Each thread block handles: - A tile of output channels: e.g., 32 or 64 - A tile of output spatial locations: e.g., 4x4 or 8x8 Each thread computes one or more output elements. Shared memory: - Load input tile of size (C_in_chunk, tile_H + K - 1, tile_W + K - 1). But C_in can be large (up to 832). Storing the full input tile for many channels is too much. - Better: loop over C_in in chunks. For each chunk of C_in (e.g., 16 or 32), load the corresponding input channels for the spatial tile into shared memory. Then accumulate. - Also load weight tile (C_out_tile, C_in_chunk, K, K) into shared memory. This is similar to the GEMM algorithm but with spatial dimensions. Let's estimate shared memory usage for a layer like conv1: C_in=3, C_out=64, K=7, H_out=112, W_out=112. Spatial tile: 8x8 output -> input tile 15x15. With pad, it's roughly that. C_in=3. So input shared memory = 3 * 15 * 15 * 4 bytes = 2.7KB. Weight shared memory = 64 * 3 * 7 * 7 * 4 = 37KB. Total ~40KB. That's fine (shared memory per SM is 48KB or more). For a later 3x3 conv at 14x14: C_in=480, C_out=192, K=3. Spatial tile: 4x4 output -> input tile 6x6. Input shared for C_in chunk=16: 16 * 6 * 6 * 4 = 2.3KB. Weight shared for C_out chunk=32: 32 * 16 * 3 * 3 * 4 = 18.4KB. Total ~21KB. Fine. For a 1x1 conv: K=1. Spatial tile can be larger. Input tile = tile_H x tile_W. For C_in chunk=32, tile 16x16: 32*16*16*4=32KB. Weight: 64*32*1*1*4=8KB. Total 40KB. So a generic tiled direct conv is feasible. But writing a highly optimized generic kernel is still a lot of code. To avoid shared memory complexity, we can have each thread read the input patch directly from global memory. Since threads within a warp access spatially nearby input pixels, caching will help. Let's break it down: **1x1 Convolution via addmm:** Input: (B, C_in, H, W) Weight: (C_out, C_in, 1, 1) -> reshape to (C_out, C_in) Bias: (C_out,) Output slice: (B, C_out, H, W) We can reshape input to (B*H*W, C_in). In PyTorch, `torch.addmm(bias, input_reshaped, weight_t)` works. The output shape is (B*H*W, C_out). Then we can view it back to (B, C_out, H, W). We can do: ```cpp auto out_view = output.slice(1, c_start, c_end).view({B*H*W, C_out}); auto in_view = input.view({B*H*W, C_in}); auto w = weight.view({C_out, C_in}); auto b = bias.view({1, C_out}); // or {C_out} at::addmm_out(out_view, b, in_view, w.t(), 1.0, 1.0); ``` Wait, `addmm_out(Tensor& out, const Tensor& self, const Tensor& mat1, const Tensor& mat2, Scalar beta, Scalar alpha)` computes `out = beta*self + alpha*mat1*mat2`. So we can set `self` to bias (broadcasted), `mat1 = in_view`, `mat2 = w.t()`, `beta=1, alpha=1`. But bias needs to be shape (1, C_out) or (C_out,). ATen will broadcast it. This avoids allocating a temporary for the conv output and writes directly into the concatenated output tensor (or any pre-allocated tensor). K=192*9=1728. col=7840*1728*4 = 54.2MB. - 3x3 at 14x14: M=1960. K=480*9=4320. col=1960*4320*4 = 33.9MB. - 3x3 at 7x7: M=490. K=832*9=7488. col=490*7488*4 = 14.7MB. 5x5 at 28x28: K=192*25=4800. M=7840. col=7840*4800*4=150MB. That's large. 5x5 at 14x14: M=1960. K=480*25=12000. col=1960*12000*4=94MB. 5x5 at 7x7: M=490. K=832*25=20800. col=490*20800*4=40.8MB. So using im2col for 5x5 convs uses a lot of memory. We can also handle them with direct conv or call `at::conv2d`. Given these large temporary memory requirements, maybe we should not use im2col for 5x5 and large 3x3, but instead call `at::conv2d` for those, and use `addmm_out` for 1x1 convs. But we still want to avoid `torch.cat` copies. So the strategy: 1. For all 1x1 convs (including reductions and projections), use `at::addmm_out` into pre-allocated output slices. 2. For all other convs (7x7, 3x3, 5x5), call `at::conv2d` (or `at::convolution_out` if possible, otherwise regular and copy). Since 1x1 convs are a huge fraction of the compute and parameters, optimizing them might help. But are 1x1 convs the bottleneck? Let's check FLOPs: - 3a 1x1: 9.6M; pool 1x1: 1.7M; reduce 1x1s: 14.4M + 0.86M. 1x1 total = 26.6M out of 144M (~18%). - 3b: 1x1 total = 256*128 + 256*128 + 256*32 + 256*64 = 32k * (128+128+32+64?) Wait per pixel: 256*128 + 256*128 + 256*32 + 256*64 = 256*(128+128+32+64)=256*352=90k per pixel. Times 784 = 70.7M. Total FLOPs ~200M. So 1x1 is ~35%. - 4a: 1x1s: 480*192 + 480*96 + 480*16 + 480*64 = 480*368 = 176k per pixel. Times 196 = 34.5M. Total maybe 300M. So 1x1 is ~11%. - 4b: 512*160 + 512*112 + 512*24 + 512*64 = 512*360 = 184k pp. *196 = 36M. Total ~350M. ~10%. - 4c: similar. - 4d: 512*112 + 512*144 + 512*32 + 512*64 = 512*352 = 180k pp. *196 = 35M. - 4e: 528*256 + 528*160 + 528*32 + 528*128 = 528*576 = 304k pp. *196 = 59.6M. Total maybe 500M. ~12%. - 5a: 832*256 + 832*160 + 832*32 + 832*128 = 832*576 = 479k pp. *49 = 23.5M. Total maybe 400M. ~6%. - 5b: 832*384 + 832*192 + 832*48 + 832*128 = 832*752 = 626k pp. *49 = 30.7M. Total maybe 600M. ~5%. So 1x1 convs are not the majority of FLOPs. The 3x3 and 5x5 dominate. But 1x1 convs are easy to optimize. However, memory bandwidth for writing intermediate branch outputs is also an issue. If we can write all branches into one contiguous output without a separate `torch.cat`, that saves memory traffic. But we still need the branch outputs to be computed. What if we write a custom CUDA kernel for the entire Inception module that uses `wmma`? No. Another idea: Since the network is relatively small, maybe the reference runtime is dominated by the final FC layer? No, FC is 1M FLOPs. Negligible. Maybe the reference is slow because of the Python `Sequential` and many intermediate tensor allocations in Inception modules. In C++: - We can allocate one output tensor per inception module. - For each branch, we call `at::conv2d` (which allocates its own output) and then copy into the slice. This is few allocations but still copies. - If we use `at::convolution_out`, we can write directly into the slice. Let's investigate if `at::convolution_out` exists. In PyTorch's `aten/src/ATen/native/Convolution.cpp`, there is: ```cpp Tensor convolution( const Tensor& input, const Tensor& weight, const Tensor& bias, IntArrayRef stride, IntArrayRef padding, IntArrayRef dilation, bool transposed, IntArrayRef output_padding, int64_t groups) { ... } ``` And there is a `_convolution` and `convolution`. I don't recall an `_out` variant. Actually, there is `Tensor& convolution_out(Tensor& out, ...)`? Let's check the ATen generated headers. Typically, PyTorch generates `at::convolution` and `at::conv2d`. The `_out` variants are generated for ops that have `Tensor& out` in their native declaration. Looking at `native_functions.yaml`, I see `convolution` does not have an `out` variant. `conv2d` also does not. `convolution` is one of the ops that doesn't support out. So we can't directly write into a slice with `at::conv2d`. But there is a trick: We can use `at::convolution` and then use `at::copy_` into the slice. This adds a copy. But the reference already has a copy in `torch.cat`. Actually, in the reference: ```python branch1x1 = self.branch1x1(x) ... return torch.cat(outputs, 1) ``` PyTorch's `torch.cat` for channel dimension when all tensors are of shape (B, C_i, H, W) will allocate a new contiguous tensor and copy each input into it. So reference does: allocate branch1x1, write conv result into it. allocate cat output, read branch1x1 and write into cat output. That's one write and one read+write per branch. If we do `branch1x1 = at::conv2d(...); output_slice.copy_(branch1x1);`, that's: write conv into branch1x1 temp, then read branch1x1 and write into output_slice. That's the same memory traffic as reference. So no memory bandwidth saving. To save, we need to write directly into the output slice. Since we can't do that with `at::conv2d`, we need custom conv kernels. So we are back to custom conv kernels. Given the time, maybe we should write custom direct conv kernels for 1x1, 3x3, 5x5, and maxpool. For the stem, we can also write one. Since the sizes are fixed, we can specialize. Let's try to estimate the effort. We need to write CUDA kernels for: - 1x1 conv + bias + relu (used many times) - 3x3 conv + bias + relu (used many times) - 5x5 conv + bias + relu (used many times) - 7x7 conv + bias + relu (conv1 only) - MaxPool 3x3 s2 p1 (used 4 times) - MaxPool 3x3 s1 p1 (used in pool branch of inception) - AdaptiveAvgPool2d to 1x1 (used once) - FC (we can leave or write a simple gemv) Plus we need to handle concatenation (just writing to different channel ranges). Writing a generic and efficient direct conv is hard but maybe we can write a moderately efficient one that's good enough because we save memory traffic. Actually, for 3x3 and 5x5, the compute is small enough that a naive loop kernel might be okay? Let's see: for 3b 3x3 conv (128->192, 3x3, 28x28), total multiply-adds = 28*28*192*128*9 = 55M. If we have 200k threads (28*28*192=150k), each doing 128*9=1152 multiply-adds. That's maybe okay. But reading weights: each thread reads 128*9 floats = 4.6KB from global memory. With 150k threads, that's 675MB of weight reads. But the weights are only 128*128*9 = 147k floats = 0.6MB. So caching will help a lot: multiple threads read the same weights. If we put weights in shared memory or rely on L1 cache, we can reduce this. But a naive kernel where each thread reads weights directly from global memory might still be okay because the weight size is small and will stay in L2 cache. However, reading input: each thread reads 128*9 input values. 150k threads * 1152 input floats = 675MB of input reads. Input size is 128*28*28 = 100k floats = 0.4MB. So again, reading the same input values repeatedly is inefficient but maybe L2 cache handles it. But threads in different blocks might not share the L1, and L2 bandwidth might become a bottleneck. A better approach: load weights and input tiles into shared memory. Let's write a generic direct conv kernel that uses shared memory tiles. Since this is a standard exercise, we can write one kernel that is templated or takes parameters, and call it for all layers. Consider the following design: ```cpp template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv2d_forward_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ input, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int H, int W, int C_out, int K, int pad, int stride, int H_out, int W_out, bool use_relu) { // tile over output channels and spatial dimensions } ``` But handling all sizes generically while using shared memory is tricky because we need compile-time sizes for arrays. We can use dynamic shared memory. Simpler approach: assign each warp to compute one output channel for a tile of spatial pixels. Or each block handles a set of output channels (e.g., 16 or 32) and a tile of spatial pixels (e.g., 8x8). Then within the block, threads load the corresponding input tile and weights cooperatively. Since the channel counts vary, we can set block dimensions as (256) and compute： - Each block handles `BLOCK_OC` output channels and `BLOCK_OW * BLOCK_OH` output pixels. - Let's say `BLOCK_OC = 32`, `BLOCK_OW = 4`, `BLOCK_OH = 4`. Then 32*16 = 512 output values per block. 256 threads means each thread computes 2 output values. Then each block iterates over C_in in chunks of `BLOCK_IC` (e.g., 32 or 16). For each chunk: - Load weight: `BLOCK_OC` x `BLOCK_IC` x `K` x `K` into shared memory. - Load input: for the spatial tile plus padding, the needed input region is `(BLOCK_OH * stride + K - 1) x (BLOCK_OW * stride + K - 1)` across `BLOCK_IC` channels. But `K` can be 1, 3, 5, 7. So the input tile size varies. We can compute the bounds dynamically. This is doable. Let's formalize: ```cpp constexpr int BLOCK_OC = 32; constexpr int BLOCK_IC = 16; // chunk of input channels // spatial tile: each thread handles one or more output pixels // Let's say each block handles a 8x8 tile of output pixels constexpr int BLOCK_OH = 8; constexpr int BLOCK_OW = 8; ``` Total output values per block = BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_OH * BLOCK_OW = 32*64 = 2048. With 256 threads, each thread computes 8 output values. Shared memory: - Weight: BLOCK_OC * BLOCK_IC * K * K. For K=5, max weight = 32*16*25 = 12800 floats = 51KB. - Input: BLOCK_IC * (BLOCK_OH * stride + K - 1) * (BLOCK_OW * stride + K - 1). For stride=1, K=5: 16 * 12 * 12 = 2304 floats = 9KB. For stride=2, K=7: 16 * 22 * 22 = 7744 floats = 31KB. Total shared memory: up to ~82KB. That's above 48KB on many GPUs. We can reduce BLOCK_IC or spatial tile. We can set: - BLOCK_OC = 16 - BLOCK_IC = 8 - BLOCK_OH = 4 - BLOCK_OW = 8 Output values = 16 * 32 = 512. With 128 threads, each thread handles 4. Shared mem weight = 16*8*25 = 3200 floats = 12.8KB. Shared mem input = 8 * (4+4) * (8+4) for K=5 stride1 = 8*8*12 = 768 floats = 3KB. For K=7 stride2: 8*(4+6)*(8+6)=8*10*14=1120f=4.5KB. Total < 20KB. Good. But with such small tiles, the number of blocks might be large, which is okay. We need to ensure good occupancy. 128 threads, ~20KB shared mem => up to 2 blocks per SM (if 48KB). Occupancy okay. However, K can be up to 7. If stride=2, output tile 4x8 corresponds to input tile (4*2+6) x (8*2+6) = 14 x 22. BC=8 => 8*14*22=2464f=9.9KB. Weight: 16*8*49=6272f=25KB. Total 35KB. Still okay for 2 blocks per SM. Or 3 blocks if 96KB shared mem (A100 has configurable 48/96/164KB). Actually A100 has up to 164KB shared memory per SM. So 35KB allows up to 4 blocks. Let's target A100? The GPU specs will tell us. Let's call `get_gpu_specs` to know what we are optimizing for.
