Actually, let me think about whether writing a single inception kernel per module is better than separate branch kernels. A single kernel means: - Shared input loading (can load input once in shared memory, reuse for all branches) - Direct output to concatenated tensor - No intermediate allocations This is a significant win! For an inception module with input [B, C, H, W] and output [B, C_out, H, W]: - Input: shared memory tile - For each output position and each output channel: - Determine which branch it belongs to - Compute the appropriate convolution - Write directly to output This requires all branches to have the same spatial dimensions, which they do (thanks to padding). The key challenge is that different branches have different filter sizes and channel counts, but we can handle this with conditionals in the kernel or by launching separate blocks for each branch. Let me design the kernel structure: For an inception module with branches: - Branch 0: C_in -> C0, 1x1 conv - Branch 1: C_in -> C1_reduce -> C1, (1x1 + 3x3) - Branch 2: C_in -> C2_reduce -> C2, (1x1 + 5x5) - Branch 3: maxpool3x3(s=1,p=1) -> C_in -> C3, (pool + 1x1) For the fused kernel, I can launch with grid covering [B, C_out, H, W]. Each thread computes one output element. The thread determines its branch based on the output channel, then applies the appropriate convolution. For shared memory optimization: - Load a tile of input into shared memory - Threads collaborate on loading input - Each thread reads from shared memory for its convolution But wait, different branches have different receptive fields. For 1x1 it's just one pixel, for 3x3 it's 3x3, for 5x5 it's 5x5. If I tile the output spatially, the input tile needs to be large enough for the 5x5 branch. For H=28, W=28 and a tile of say 16x16, the input tile would need to be 20x20 for 5x5 with padding=2. That's 400 elements * C_in channels. If C_in = 192, that's 76,800 floats = ~300 KB. Shared memory is 227KB per block max. So I'd need to load per-channel or use smaller tiles. If I process one channel at a time for each output pixel: load all C_in channels for the needed spatial region into shared memory. But that might be too much. Alternative: don't use shared memory for input, just rely on L2 cache. Since the same input is read by all branches, and we're processing the whole feature map, the input should stay in cache as long as all branches are processed together. For L2 cache effectiveness, it's better to process all output channels for a given spatial location together, or process all spatial locations for a branch together. But the former (all channels for one spatial location) has better locality for the input. Hmm, let me think about a simpler but still effective approach. **Simpler approach: Fused branch-wise processing with sequential memory access** For each inception module: 1. Instead of separate PyTorch conv calls that allocate intermediate buffers, have a single kernel that processes all branches 2. But the kernel can be structured as: iterate over output channels, and for each output channel group (branch), apply the corresponding convolution Or even simpler: have separate kernels for each branch type, but they all write into different regions of a pre-allocated output buffer. And the input is shared. 1. Pre-allocate single output buffer of size [B, C_out, H, W] 2. Launch kernel for branch 1x1 that writes to output[:, 0:C0, :, :] 3. Launch kernel for branch 3x3 that writes to output[:, C0:C0+C1, :, :] 4. Launch kernel for branch 5x5 that writes to output[:, C0+C1:C0+C1+C2, :, :] 5. Launch kernel for branch pool that writes to output[:, C0+C1+C2:C0+C1+C2+C3, :, :] This way we avoid concat allocation overhead, but we still launch 4 kernels per inception module. That's 4 * 9 = 36 kernels just for inception modules, plus more for other layers. Total might be ~50 kernels. PyTorch's Inception module launches: 1 + 2 + 2 + 2 = 7 convs + 1 maxpool + 1 concat = ~11 ops per module. For 9 modules that's 99 ops. Plus early layers, late layers, etc. Maybe 120+ kernel launches total. By reducing the number of operations (fusing ReLU into convs, fusing reduce+conv into single kernels where possible, avoiding concat), I can reduce to maybe 60-70 kernel launches. This should help with overhead. But the real win would be fewer than ~50 kernel launches for the whole forward pass. Let me think about what operations can be fused into single kernels: - conv + relu: fuse into conv kernel - reduce_1x1 + relu + conv_3x3 + relu: can this be fused? Yes! Compute 1x1 conv, apply relu, then immediately use as input to 3x3 conv, all in registers/shared memory. This avoids writing the intermediate to global memory. This is huge! In PyTorch: reduce conv -> write to global -> relu -> write to global -> 3x3 conv -> write to global -> relu -> write to global. That's at least 4 memory passes. Fused: read input -> compute -> write output once. Much better! Similarly for 5x5 branch. Similarly for pool branch: maxpool -> conv1x1 -> relu, all fused. And for early layers: conv1 -> relu -> maxpool -> conv2 -> relu -> conv3 -> relu -> maxpool, potentially all fused? But conv3 needs the output of conv2 across the whole feature map, so they can't be fully fused into one kernel unless we tile appropriately. For 1x1 conv -> 3x3 conv with pad=1: if we tile the output spatially, we can load an input tile, compute the 1x1 conv (same spatial size), then use the result to compute the 3x3 conv (also same spatial size, since pad=1), all while keeping data in shared memory or registers. This is feasible! For sequential convs where the first is 1x1 and the second is kxk with padding=(k-1)/2: - 1x1 conv preserves spatial dimensions - kxk conv with same padding also preserves spatial dimensions - So we can tile spatially and process each tile independently! This means we can fuse: - 1x1 reduce -> relu -> 3x3 conv -> relu - 1x1 reduce -> relu -> 5x5 conv -> relu Into single kernels per output tile! For the inception module, if we fuse the sequential convs in branches, we get: - Branch 0: 1x1 conv + relu - Branch 1: 1x1 reduce + relu + 3x3 + relu (fused) - Branch 2: 1x1 reduce + relu + 5x5 + relu (fused) - Branch 3: maxpool3x3 + 1x1 + relu (fused) Then we just need to launch these 4 kernels per inception module, each writing to a subrange of the output buffer. Further, if we pre-allocate output buffers and reuse them between layers, we can minimize allocation overhead. For the first few layers before inception modules: - conv1(7x7,s2) + relu + maxpool(s2) - conv2(1x1) + relu - conv3(3x3,p1) + relu + maxpool(s2) conv1 + relu + maxpool can be fused into one kernel. conv2 is standalone 1x1. conv3 + relu + maxpool can be fused. Let me count kernels for full forward pass with this approach: 1. conv1_7x7_relu_maxpool 2. conv2_1x1_relu 3. conv3_3x3_relu_maxpool 4. incep3a_branch0_1x1_relu 5. incep3a_branch1_fused 6. incep3a_branch2_fused 7. incep3a_branch3_fused 8. incep3b_branch0_1x1_relu 9. incep3b_branch1_fused 10. incep3b_branch2_fused 11. incep3b_branch3_fused 12. maxpool3 13. incep4a_branch0... (4 branches * 5 modules) + maxpool4 = 20 + 1 = 21 Then 5a, 5b = 8 Final avgpool + flatten + dropout + fc = 1 (or use PyTorch for FC) Total: 3 + 8 + 1 + 21 + 8 + 1 = ~42 kernels. Much better than PyTorch's ~120+. And many of these are fused, reducing memory bandwidth. Now let me think about implementing the conv kernels. **1x1 conv + bias + ReLU kernel:** This is the simplest. Each output element [b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} W[c_out, c_in] * x[b, c_in, h, w] + b[c_out], then relu. We can implement this with each block processing a tile of [C_out_tile, HW_tile] and loading a tile of C_in into shared memory. Template: ``` __global__ void conv1x1_relu( float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) ``` Tiling strategy: - BlockDim: (32, 8, 1) or something similar - Each block processes 32 output channels and 8*HW spatial points? Or process spatial in 2D. Actually, for 1x1 conv, the spatial dimensions are independent. We can treat it as a batched GEMM: - M = B * H * W (number of "pixels") - K = C_in - N = C_out Using a standard GEMM kernel with tiling. For simplicity and effectiveness, I can write a shared-memory tiled kernel: - Each thread block handles a tile of output channels and spatial locations - Load C_in chunk into shared memory - Compute partial dot products - Accumulate and write with ReLU **Direct kxk conv + bias + ReLU kernel:** For 3x3 and 5x5 convs with padding, each output pixel depends on a kxk neighborhood. Kernel: ``` __global__ void convkxk_relu( float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W, int k) ``` Tiling: - Tile in output spatial and output channel dimensions - Input tile needs to be expanded by (k-1) in each spatial dimension - Load input tile into shared memory, pad with zeros at boundaries - Each thread computes one or more output elements For small feature maps (7x7, 14x14) and reasonable channels, a thread can compute one output element directly by reading from global memory if we ensure coalesced access. Actually, let me think about register-based approaches. For 3x3 conv with C_in input channels and C_out output channels: - For each output channel and spatial position, we need to read a 3x3xC_in region - If we process one output pixel per thread, and the threads in a warp process adjacent pixels, consecutive threads will read adjacent memory (good coalescing) - But each thread needs to read 9*C_in values, which is a lot of global memory traffic Using shared memory helps: load a spatial tile into shared memory, then threads read from there. For 14x14 with 3x3 conv, a 16x16 output tile requires 18x18 input tile. If channels are processed in groups (e.g., 8 channels at a time), the shared memory usage is manageable. **Fused sequential conv kernel (1x1 + relu + kxk + relu):** This is basically two convs in sequence. We can: 1. Load input spatial tile to shared memory 2. Compute 1x1 conv to produce intermediate tile in shared memory or registers 3. Apply ReLU to intermediate 4. Use intermediate to compute kxk conv 5. Apply ReLU to output and write to global memory Since 1x1 conv preserves spatial size and kxk conv with padding also preserves size, the spatial tiling works perfectly. **Fused maxpool + 1x1 conv + relu (for pool branch):** For 3x3 maxpool with stride=1, pad=1: - Each output pixel is max of 3x3 neighborhood - Followed by 1x1 conv - Can fuse: for each output channel and pixel, compute maxpool on the fly, then multiply by 1x1 weights Since maxpool here preserves spatial dimensions and each branch has C_in -> C_out channels, we can compute it per-thread: read the 3x3 neighborhood, take max, multiply by weights. For the pool branch in inception, it's maxpool then 1x1 conv. The maxpool is stride=1, pad=1 (same output size). So for each output pixel: 1. Compute max over 3x3 input neighborhood 2. Multiply by 1x1 weight and add bias 3. Apply ReLU This is very efficient! Each thread only needs to read 9 input values (actually fewer with tiling), compute max, then do a dot product with weights. **conv1_7x7_stride2_relu + maxpool3_stride2:** This is the first block. Input 3x224x224 -> conv 7x7, stride 2, pad 3 -> 64x112x112 -> relu -> maxpool 3x3, stride 2, pad 1 -> 64x56x56. For this, each output pixel (56x56) depends on a ~14x14 input region (due to stride 2 conv and stride 2 pool). That's a lot of input data per output pixel. Actually, let's trace it: - Conv stride 2: output (h, w) depends on input [2h-3 : 2h+4, 2w-3 : 2w+4] (7x7 region) - Maxpool stride 2, 3x3: output (h', w') = max over conv output [2h'-1 : 2h'+2, 2w'-1 : 2w'+2] - Combined: output (h', w') depends on input roughly [4h'-5 : 4h'+8, 4w'-5 : 4w'+8], which is ~14x14 This is complex to fuse. Maybe I should keep conv1 and maxpool1 separate, or use a simpler kernel. Wait, actually I can think of it differently. The output is 56x56, and each output pixel is computed independently. For each output pixel, I need to: 1. Determine which 7x7 conv outputs map to its maxpool 2. For each such conv output, compute the conv value and track the max The maxpool stride 2, 3x3, pad 1 on a 112x112 feature map: - Output size = floor((112 + 2*1 - 3)/2) + 1 = 56 - Output (i, j) looks at conv outputs: - h_start = max(0, 2*i - 1), h_end = min(111, 2*i + 1) - w_start = max(0, 2*j - 1), w_end = min(111, 2*j + 1) - So it's a 2x2 or 3x3 region of conv outputs For each conv output in that region, compute the 7x7 conv, and take the max. So for batch_size=10, C_out=64, output 56x56: total output elements = 10*64*56*56 = 1,996,800. Each element requires computing 1-4 conv values, each conv value is a 7x7x3 dot product = 147 multiplies. That's a lot of redundant computation! For the maxpool overlap, conv values would be recomputed multiple times. Better approach: compute conv1 output first, then maxpool. But conv1 output is 10*64*112*112 = 7,997,440 elements. If we can keep it in registers/shared memory... not feasible. So either: 1. Write conv1 output to global memory, then maxpool (PyTorch style, but with fused relu) 2. Or accept redundant computation for full fusion For simplicity, I'll use approach 1: separate conv1 + relu kernel, then maxpool kernel. But I can fuse the ReLU into conv1, saving one memory pass. Similarly for conv3 + relu + maxpool2: conv3 output is 10*192*28*28 = 1,505,280 elements. Again, writing intermediate is fine, just fuse relu into conv. Actually, given the difficulty of beating cuDNN on large convolutions, maybe I should take a hybrid approach: 1. Use PyTorch's conv2d for the large initial convolutions (conv1, conv3) 2. But implement custom kernels for everything else and fuse aggressively But the task says "replace the forward computation with a custom CUDA kernel." It doesn't say I can't use PyTorch inside for specific operations... but to get a good speedup, I should probably do most of it custom. Actually, wait. Let me reconsider. The 1x1 convs in the inception modules are probably the most common operation, and they're also relatively easy to write custom kernels for. The 3x3 and 5x5 convs are harder but still manageable. The initial 7x7 and 3x3 convs are larger and cuDNN likely does very well on them. One very pragmatic approach: keep conv1, conv3 as PyTorch ops (with relu), but implement the inception modules as fully fused custom kernels. The inception modules contain many small convs that might not saturate the GPU well individually, so fusing them and batching the work could provide speedup. Actually, in PyTorch, small convolutions are already handled reasonably well by cuDNN. But the overhead of launching many small kernels and the memory bandwidth of intermediate results is the bottleneck. Let me try a middle ground: 1. Use custom kernels for 1x1 convs (very easy to implement well) 2. Use custom fused kernels for 1x1->3x3 and 1x1->5x5 sequences 3. Use custom kernel for pool+1x1 4. Use PyTorch for conv1 and conv3 if my custom versions aren't faster, otherwise use custom 5. Implement maxpool as separate custom kernels or fused where possible For the initial convs, I'll try writing custom versions, but if they don't help, I can fall back to PyTorch. Wait, no - I shouldn't use PyTorch conv2d if I'm trying to write a custom kernel. Let me just write everything custom. Let's start designing the CUDA code. **Kernel 1: conv1x1_relu** ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_relu(float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { // Each thread computes one output element int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H * W; if (idx >= total) return; // Decompose idx into (b, c, h, w) int w = idx % W; int tmp = idx / W; int h = tmp % H; tmp = tmp / H; int c = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; // Compute dot product float sum = bias[c]; int in_base = ((b * C_in + 0) * H + h) * W + w; int w_base = c * C_in; for (int i = 0; i < C_in; i++) { sum += in[in_base + i * H * W] * weight[w_base + i]; } out[idx] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This is simple but may not be optimal. Better to use shared memory for weights and coarser parallelism. Actually, for 1x1 conv, the weight matrix is [C_out, C_in]. If we tile in C_out and process multiple spatial points per thread, we can get good memory coalescing. Let me design a better version: ```cuda #define TILE_C_OUT 64 #define TILE_HW 16 __global__ void conv1x1_relu(float* out, const float* in, const float* weight, const float* bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { __shared__ float s_weight[TILE_C_OUT][TILE_C_IN]; // weight tile __shared__ float s_in[TILE_HW][TILE_C_IN]; // input tile int hw = H * W; int block_c_out = blockIdx.x * TILE_C_OUT; int block_hw = blockIdx.y * TILE_HW; int b = blockIdx.z; float accum[TILE_C_OUT / 32]; // each warp handles 32 output channels // Initialize accumulators for (int i = 0; i < TILE_C_OUT / 32; i++) { accum[i] = 0; } // Loop over C_in in tiles for (int c_in_tile = 0; c_in_tile < C_in; c_in_tile += TILE_C_IN) { // Load weight tile [TILE_C_OUT, TILE_C_IN] into shared memory // Load input tile [TILE_HW, TILE_C_IN] into shared memory // Compute partial sums for (int i = 0; i < TILE_C_OUT / 32; i++) { for (int j = 0; j < TILE_C_IN; j++) { accum[i] += s_weight[threadIdx.x + i*32][j] * s_in[threadIdx.y][j]; } } } // Write output with ReLU } ``` This is getting complicated. Given time constraints, let me use a simpler but still effective approach. I can use a warp-level GEMM approach for 1x1 conv. Actually, there's an even simpler approach that works well for many channel configurations: use one thread per output pixel, and have threads in a block collaborate to load input channels into shared memory. For 1x1 conv with C_in input channels and C_out output channels: - Launch with block = (256, 1, 1) or similar - Each block processes a group of output channels and spatial locations - For a group of 8 output channels, 32 spatial locations: 8*32 = 256 threads - Process 8 output channels at once by loading C_in input channels - This requires C_in * 32 * sizeof(float) shared memory for input If C_in = 192, that's 192 * 32 * 4 = 24,576 bytes = 24 KB. Very reasonable! If C_in = 512, that's 512 * 32 * 4 = 65,536 bytes = 64 KB. Still reasonable. Let me code this up: ```cuda // 1x1 conv + ReLU // Block: (32, 8, 1) -> 256 threads // Grid: (C_out / 8, (H*W) / 32, B) // Each block processes 8 output channels and 32 spatial locations // Threads in x-dim handle spatial, y-dim handles output channels __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_PER_BLOCK = 32; const int C_OUT_PER_BLOCK = 8; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_PER_BLOCK][C_IN_TILE]; // too variable... ``` Hmm, using dynamic shared memory is better. But even with fixed sizes, we can template the kernel. Actually, let me template by C_in to get better register usage and shared memory sizes. But the model has many different C_in values: 3, 64, 96, 128, 192, 256, etc. That's too many for templating. Alternative: loop over C_in in chunks of, say, 32 or 64. Let me write a general kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_base = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_base = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; // 0 to 7 int local_hw = threadIdx.x; // 0 to 31 int hw = H * W; int global_hw = hw_base + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_base + local_c_out; if (global_c_out >= C_out || global_hw >= hw) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; float sum = 0.0f; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE][C_IN_TILE]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE][C_IN_TILE]; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load input tile into shared memory // Each thread loads one element int c_in = c_in_base + local_c_out; // reuse y-dim for loading if (c_in < C_in && local_hw < SPATIAL_TILE) { int in_idx = ((b * C_in + c_in) * H + h) * W + w; // Hmm, this loads the same spatial position for different c_in // But we need to load contiguous c_in for the same spatial position } } ``` This is getting messy with the memory layout. Let me think about this more carefully. Input layout: [B, C_in, H, W] in NCHW format. For a fixed (b, h, w), the C_in channels are contiguous in memory. For a fixed (b, c_in), the H*W spatial positions are contiguous. In PyTorch, the default is NCHW. So `in[b, c, h, w]` is at `in[((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w]`. For coalesced access: - Threads in a warp with adjacent `threadIdx.x` should access adjacent memory. - If we map `threadIdx.x` to spatial (hw) and `threadIdx.y` to output channels, then for loading weights, threads with different `threadIdx.y` access different weight rows - not coalesced. - For loading input, threads with different `threadIdx.x` access different spatial positions - these are contiguous if we access the same channel. Good. Let me redesign: - `threadIdx.x` (0-31): handles 32 consecutive spatial positions - `threadIdx.y` (0-7): handles 8 output channels For loading weights: all threads need weight[global_c_out, c_in] for their global_c_out. Threads with same `threadIdx.y` share the same global_c_out. To load weights coalesced, we should have threads with adjacent `threadIdx.x` load adjacent `c_in` for the same `c_out`. For loading input: threads with adjacent `threadIdx.x` load adjacent spatial positions for the same `c_in`. Coalesced! Let me try again: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 64; // must be >= blockDim.x int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int tid_x = threadIdx.x; // 0 to 31, handles spatial int tid_y = threadIdx.y; // 0 to 7, handles output channels int hw = H * W; int global_hw = hw_block + tid_x; int global_c_out = c_out_block + tid_y; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE][C_IN_TILE]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE][C_IN_TILE]; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load input: [SPATIAL_TILE, C_IN_TILE] // Each thread loads one (spatial, c_in) pair int c_in = c_in_base + tid_x; // Wait, tid_x is 0-31, C_IN_TILE=64 // So we need each thread to load 2 elements, or use more threads ``` This is getting complicated. Let me use a simpler approach: have each thread load one input element and one weight element per tile. For input with C_IN_TILE=32 and SPATIAL_TILE=32: - We need 32*32 = 1024 floats. - Block has 256 threads. Each thread loads 4 floats. For weights with C_OUT_TILE=8 and C_IN_TILE=32: - We need 8*32 = 256 floats. - Each thread loads 1 float. Let me simplify the indexing: ```cuda __global__ void conv1x1_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int tid = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * blockDim.x; // 0 to 255 int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; // 0 to 7 int local_hw = threadIdx.x; // 0 to 31 int hw = H * W; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE * C_IN_TILE]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; for (int c_base = 0; c_base < C_in; c_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Collaborative load of input tile // We need s_in[local_hw][c_in_local] = in[b][c_base + c_in_local][h][w] // But h and w depend on local_hw! So different threads load different spatial positions // Each thread loads C_IN_TILE / (some division) elements // Let's have each thread load C_IN_TILE / 8 = 4 input elements for (int i = 0; i < C_IN_TILE / 8; i++) { int c_in_local = local_c_out * (C_IN_TILE / 8) + i; if (c_base + c_in_local < C_in) { int in_addr = ((b * C_in + c_base + c_in_local) * H + h) * W + w; s_in[local_hw * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; } } // Collaborative load of weight tile // Each thread loads 1 weight element int w_load_idx = tid; if (w_load_idx < C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE) { int w_c_out = w_load_idx / C_IN_TILE; int w_c_in = w_load_idx % C_IN_TILE; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; int global_w_c_in = c_base + w_c_in; if (global_w_c_out < C_out && global_w_c_in < C_in) { s_weight[w_load_idx] = weight[global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in]; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute partial dot product #pragma unroll for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE; c_in_local++) { if (c_base + c_in_local < C_in) { sum += s_in[local_hw * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local] * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]; } } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This is still not ideal because: 1. Input loading: threads with adjacent local_hw access non-contiguous memory (different h,w positions). This is bad for coalescing. 2. Wait, actually, for a fixed (b, c_in), different (h,w) are contiguous in memory. So if threads with adjacent local_hw read the same channel but different spatial positions, they ARE reading contiguous memory. Good. But in my code, thread with local_hw=0 reads in[b][c_in][h0][w0] and thread with local_hw=1 reads in[b][c_in][h1][w1]. Since h and w are derived from global_hw = hw_block + local_hw, adjacent local_hw means adjacent global_hw, which means adjacent memory. Good, coalesced! For weight loading: 256 threads loading 256 floats, each thread one float. The weight index is global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in. Are adjacent threads loading adjacent memory? tid ranges 0-255. w_load_idx = tid. So thread 0 loads weight[0], thread 1 loads weight[1], etc. Yes, contiguous! Coalesced! For computation: threads read from shared memory. s_in[local_hw * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local] - for fixed c_in_local, adjacent local_hw read different rows of s_in. s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local] - threads with same local_c_out read same row. No bank conflicts if we're careful (but there might be broadcast or 8-way conflict here). Actually, s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]: all threads in a warp have the same local_c_out? No, local_c_out = threadIdx.y. In a warp of 32 threads, threadIdx.x varies from 0-31 and threadIdx.y varies from 0-7? Wait, how are warps partitioned? With blockDim=(32, 8), threads are laid out as (x=0..31, y=0..7). A warp is 32 threads. Threads with the same (y) and adjacent (x) are in the same warp? No, in CUDA, warps are filled in x-dimension first. So warp 0 has threads (x=0..31, y=0). Warp 1 has (x=0..31, y=1). Etc. So each warp has 32 threads with the same local_c_out. When threads read s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local], all threads in the warp read the same address. This is a broadcast, no bank conflict. Good! When threads read s_in[local_hw * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local], threads in the same warp have different local_hw (0 to 31). They read addresses: 0*32 + c, 1*32 + c, ..., 31*32 + c. If shared memory has 32 banks and each bank is 4 bytes, then these accesses are to banks: (0*32 + c)%32 = c%32, (1*32 + c)%32 = c%32, ..., (31*32 + c)%32 = c%32. All threads in the warp read the SAME bank! This is a 32-way bank conflict! To fix this, I need to pad or reorder the shared memory layout. For example, use `s_in[SPATIAL_TILE][C_IN_TILE + 1]` to avoid conflicts, or change the access pattern. Actually, looking more carefully: if C_IN_TILE = 32, and shared memory has 32 banks of 4 bytes each, then: - s_in[0][c] is at bank c - s_in[1][c] is at bank (32 + c) % 32 = c - s_in[2][c] is at bank (64 + c) % 32 = c - etc. Yes, all threads in the warp read the same bank. Terrible! Solution: use `s_in[SPATIAL_TILE][C_IN_TILE + 1]`. Then: - s_in[0][c] is at bank c - s_in[1][c] is at bank (33 + c) % 32 = (c + 1) % 32 - s_in[2][c] is at bank (66 + c) % 32 = (c + 2) % 32 - etc. Now threads in the warp read different banks. Perfect! Similarly for s_weight, but since threads in the same warp all read the same address, there's no conflict there. Let me update the shared memory declarations: ```cuda __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE * (C_IN_TILE + 1)]; // or __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE][C_IN_TILE + 1]; ``` Actually with linear indexing: ```cuda s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] ``` Good. Let me revise. Also, I realized the input loading has another issue. Each thread loads `(C_IN_TILE / 8) = 4` elements. For local_c_out = 0, it loads c_in_local = 0, 1, 2, 3. For local_c_out = 1, it loads c_in_local = 4, 5, 6, 7. So threads in the same warp load: - Thread (x, 0): spatial position h,w at channels 0, 1, 2, 3 - Thread (x, 1): spatial position h,w at channels 4, 5, 6, 7 Wait, but the memory layout is channel-major. So in[b][0][h][w], in[b][1][h][w], etc. are NOT contiguous! They're separated by H*W. Oh right, the input is NCHW. So in[b, c, h, w] is at offset ((b*C + c)*H + h)*W + w. For fixed b, h, w, adjacent c are separated by H*W. So my input loading is NOT coalesced! Threads in a warp load from: Thread 0: addr0 + 0*H*W Thread 1: addr1 + 0*H*W (different spatial position, same channel) Thread 32: addr0 + 4*H*W This isn't good for coalescing either. For coalesced loading of input with different c, I need threads in a warp reading the same spatial position and adjacent channels. But that means I need blockDim.x to map to channels, not spatial. Let me redesign for NCHW: For coalesced memory access, consecutive threads in x should access consecutive memory. In NCHW, the innermost dimension is W, then H, then C, then B. So for a given (b, c), adjacent w are contiguous. Adjacent h are separated by W. Adjacent c are separated by H*W. For 1x1 conv at a fixed (b, c_out, h, w), we need all c_in values for the same (b, h, w). To coalesce input loads: - Have threads in the same warp read the same (b, h, w) but different c_in - But then each thread needs to access in[((b*C_in + c_in)*H + h)*W + w] - Adjacent c_in have addresses differing by H*W If H*W is large (like 56*56 = 3136), adjacent threads are loading memory 3136*4 = 12,544 bytes apart. This is not coalesced. It will likely be strided access. But actually, modern GPUs with L2 cache might handle this OK for small channel counts. And if H*W is a multiple of cache line size, it might even be somewhat efficient. Alternatively, I could transpose the input first, or use NC/32HW32 format. But transposing costs time. Let me think about what memory access pattern actually works best. Actually, on Ampere and Hopper, the memory subsystem is very good at handling strided accesses as long as the stride isn't too large. With 128-byte cache lines and 3136-element stride, each thread touches a different cache line. A warp of 32 threads would touch 32 cache lines = 4096 bytes. But the L2 cache can handle multiple requests in parallel. For smaller feature maps (like 7x7 = 49), the stride is 196 floats = 784 bytes. Each warp touches 32 * 784 = 25,088 bytes. This is still strided. For the best performance with NCHW, maybe I should have each thread process one output element and loop sequentially over c_in? This means no shared memory for input, just registers for accumulation. The memory reads would be sequential within each thread (good for cache), and different threads accessing different spatial positions would be coalesced for the same c_in. Wait, let me reconsider. Each thread computes one (b, c_out, h, w). It loops over c_in, reading in[b, c_in, h, w] and weight[c_out, c_in]. For a warp of 32 threads computing the same (b, c_out) but different (h, w): - When c_in = 0: thread i reads in[b, 0, hi, wi]. These are contiguous if hi, wi are adjacent. Good coalescing! - weight[c_out, 0] is read by all threads. Broadcast from constant cache or L1. For a warp of 32 threads computing different c_out but same (b, h, w): - in[b, 0, h, w], in[b, 1, h, w], etc. are strided by H*W. Bad coalescing. - weight[0, c_in], weight[1, c_in], etc. are contiguous. Good coalescing. So it's better to have threads in a warp share c_out and vary hw (spatial), rather than share hw and vary c_out. With this in mind, my earlier approach where threadIdx.x maps to spatial and threadIdx.y maps to c_out is actually better for input coalescing. Let me keep that. For input loading into shared memory: We want threads in a warp to load contiguous memory from in[b, c_in, h, w]. Since adjacent x-dim threads have adjacent hw, for a fixed c_in they load contiguous memory. Good. But in my code, the loading is done with `local_c_out` partitioning the c_in dimension: ```cuda for (int i = 0; i < C_IN_TILE / C_OUT_TILE; i++) { int c_in_local = local_c_out * (C_IN_TILE / C_OUT_TILE) + i; if (c_base + c_in_local < C_in) { int in_addr = ((b * C_in + c_base + c_in_local) * H + h) * W + w; s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; } } ``` Wait, this has threads with different local_c_out loading different c_in values. For warp with local_c_out = 0, they load c_in_local = 0,1,2,3. For warp with local_c_out = 1, they load c_in_local = 4,5,6,7. Threads in warp 0 (local_c_out=0, local_hw=0..31) load: - in[b, c_base, h0, w0], in[b, c_base, h1, w1], ..., in[b, c_base, h31, w31] These are contiguous. Good! Threads in warp 0 with local_c_out=0, i=1 load: - in[b, c_base+1, h0, w0], in[b, c_base+1, h1, w1], etc. These are also contiguous (for a fixed c_in). Good! But warp 0 performs this load 4 times (i=0,1,2,3), for c_in = c_base, c_base+1, c_base+2, c_base+3. Warp 1 (local_c_out=1) loads c_in = c_base+4, c_base+5, c_base+6, c_base+7. Also contiguous. This seems fine. All memory loads are coalesced, and all warps participate in loading. But wait - I need to make sure all threads in a block participate, and the `__syncthreads()` is placed correctly. Actually, there's a problem: my thread assignment has threadIdx.y=0..7. Warp 0 is (x=0..31, y=0). Warp 1 is (x=0..31, y=1). etc. So 8 warps total. For i=0: all 256 threads load one element. Good. For i=1: all 256 threads load another element. Good. For i=2: same. For i=3: same. But we need 32*32 = 1024 elements in s_in. Each iteration loads 256 elements. 4 iterations = 1024 elements. Good. For s_weight: 8*32 = 256 elements. Each thread loads one element in one go. 256 threads. 1 iteration. Good. Then computation: all 256 threads do the reduction. Good. Then write output: each thread writes one output element. Coalesced. Good. This looks solid! Let me refine it. Wait, there's another issue. The `c_in_local` goes from 0 to 31. But `C_IN_TILE = 32`. And `local_c_out` goes from 0 to 7. So `local_c_out * 4 + i` gives: - y=0: 0, 1, 2, 3 - y=1: 4, 5, 6, 7 - ... - y=7: 28, 29, 30, 31 Total 32 values. Good. Now the s_in indexing: ```cuda s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; ``` local_hw = 0..31, c_in_local depends on local_c_out and i. For i=0 and y=0: s_in[local_hw * 33 + 0] = in[...] For i=0 and y=1: s_in[local_hw * 33 + 4] = in[...] After all loads, s_in[row][col] where row=local_hw, col=c_in_local. total columns 0..31. Good. Now computation: ```cuda for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE; c_in_local++) { sum += s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]; } ``` Check bank conflicts for s_in: Threads in warp 0 have local_hw = 0..31 (different) and local_c_out = 0 (same). They read s_in[local_hw * 33 + c_in_local]. For a fixed c_in_local, thread with local_hw=0 reads bank (0)%32 = 0. local_hw=1 reads bank (33)%32 = 1. local_hw=2 reads bank (66)%32 = 2. ... local_hw=31 reads bank (1023)%32 = 31. Perfect, no bank conflicts! For s_weight: Threads in warp 0 read s_weight[0 * 32 + c_in_local] = s_weight[c_in_local]. All same address. Broadcast. No conflict. Excellent! This kernel should work well. Let me finalize the 1x1 conv kernel: ```cuda #define SPATIAL_TILE_1x1 32 #define C_OUT_TILE_1x1 8 #define C_IN_TILE_1x1 32 __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE_1x1; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE_1x1; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; // 0 .. 31 int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; // 0 .. 7 int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE_1x1 * (C_IN_TILE_1x1 + 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE_1x1 * C_IN_TILE_1x1]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; int c_in_per_thread = C_IN_TILE_1x1 / C_OUT_TILE_1x1; // 4 for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE_1x1) { // Load input tile for (int i = 0; i < c_in_per_thread; i++) { int c_in_local = local_c_out * c_in_per_thread + i; int global_c_in = c_in_base + c_in_local; if (global_c_in < C_in) { int in_addr = ((b * C_in + global_c_in) * H + h) * W + w; s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE_1x1 + 1) + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; } } // Load weight tile int tid = local_hw + local_c_out * blockDim.x; int w_load_idx = tid; if (w_load_idx < C_OUT_TILE_1x1 * C_IN_TILE_1x1) { int w_c_out = w_load_idx / C_IN_TILE_1x1; int w_c_in = w_load_idx % C_IN_TILE_1x1; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; int global_w_c_in = c_in_base + w_c_in; if (global_w_c_out < C_out && global_w_c_in < C_in) { s_weight[w_load_idx] = weight[global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in]; } else { s_weight[w_load_idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute #pragma unroll for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE_1x1; c_in_local++) { sum += s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE_1x1 + 1) + c_in_local] * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE_1x1 + c_in_local]; } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This should be a decent 1x1 conv kernel. Now for 3x3 and 5x5 direct conv + relu. For kxk conv with padding, each output pixel depends on a kxk neighborhood. I'll write a direct convolution kernel. For the fused 1x1 -> kxk conv, the output of the 1x1 conv is consumed immediately by the kxk conv, so we can keep it in registers/shared memory. Let me think about the fused kernel more carefully. **Fused 1x1 + ReLU + 3x3 + ReLU kernel:** Input: [B, C_in, H, W] Output: [B, C_out, H, W] (same spatial size since padding=1 for 3x3) Intermediate: [H, W, C_mid] after 1x1 + relu For each output tile, we can: 1. Load input tile into shared memory 2. Compute 1x1 conv to produce intermediate (could be stored in another shared memory buffer or registers) 3. Apply ReLU to intermediate 4. Compute 3x3 conv from intermediate to output 5. Apply ReLU and write to output Challenge: intermediate full spatial tile * C_mid might be too large for shared memory. Example: H=W=28, tile=14x14, C_mid=96. - Input tile for 3x3: 16x16xC_in. If C_in=192, that's 16*16*192*4 = 196,608 bytes. - Intermediate: 14x14x96*4 = 75,264 bytes. - Output: 14x14x128*4 = 100,352 bytes. - Total: too much! Alternative: process in smaller tiles, or stream through channels. For smaller spatial dimensions, shared memory is more manageable. E.g., 7x7 output tile needs 9x9 input for 3x3. If C_in=832, that's 9*9*832*4 = 269,568 bytes. Still too much! Hmm, for large C_in and small spatial dims, shared memory might be limiting. An alternative is to process one output pixel per thread and keep the filter weights in shared memory or registers, reading input directly from global memory. For 3x3 conv with C_in input channels and C_out output channels: - Each output pixel needs 3*3*C_in input values - Each thread computes 1 output pixel for 1 output channel - 256 threads = 256 output pixels For C_in=192: each thread reads 9*192 = 1728 floats. That's a lot of memory traffic but might be OK if cached. For 5x5 conv: each thread reads 25*C_in floats. Even more. Given the complexity, let me try a different approach for kxk convs: use shared memory for the filter weights, and read input directly from global memory with good coalescing. For a thread computing output (b, c_out, h, w): - It needs input (b, c_in, h+dy, w+dx) for dy,dx in {-1,0,1} and all c_in - If I have 256 threads computing the same c_out but different (h,w): - They read in[b, c_in, :, :] at different positions - For c_in=0, thread i reads in[hi, wi], thread i+1 reads in[hi+1, wi] or similar - These are contiguous if we process adjacent w, then adjacent h Actually, for adjacent spatial positions, let's say (h, w) and (h, w+1). The 3x3 neighborhoods overlap significantly (6 out of 9 pixels). But global memory accesses for the same c_in and adjacent w are contiguous. For a warp of 32 threads computing the same c_out and adjacent (h, w): - When reading c_in=0, dy=0, dx=0: threads read in[h][w], in[h][w+1], ... which is contiguous. Good coalescing! - When reading c_in=0, dy=0, dx=1: threads read in[h][w+1], in[h][w+2], ... contiguous. Good! - etc. So if I have a warp compute a row of output pixels, the memory access pattern is very coalesced. For C_in=192 and 3x3 conv, each thread needs 1728 loads. With 32 threads and good coalescing, each warp needs 1728 * 32 * 4 bytes = 221,184 bytes of global memory reads per output channel. This might be bandwidth-limited. Shared memory doesn't help much here because different threads need different input regions (they compute different output pixels). The overlap means L1/L2 cache might help though. Alternatively, I can use a different tiling: each block computes a tile of output channels and a tile of output spatial positions. All threads in the block share the same input tile. This is the classic direct conv approach. Let me do a simpler but still effective direct conv kernel: ```cuda // Direct kxk conv + bias + ReLU // Each block computes [C_OUT_TILE] output channels for [HW_TILE] spatial positions // Block: (32, 8, 1) -> 256 threads // For 3x3 with pad=1: __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int HW_TILE = 16; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * HW_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x % HW_TILE; // Or distribute differently int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; // ... } ``` Actually, let me use a simpler approach: each thread computes ONE output element for ALL its assigned output channels. This avoids complex tiling. For 3x3 conv: - Block: (16, 16, 1) = 256 threads - Each thread handles one spatial position - Threads in the block cover a 16x16 tile of output - Each thread computes 1 output channel, or multiple if C_out is small Actually, for inception modules, C_out per branch is like 64, 128, 32, etc. So not too large. Let me use: block = (HW_PER_BLOCK, 1, 1) where HW_PER_BLOCK is like 256. Each thread computes one spatial position and loops over C_out. Wait, that's too sequential for C_out. Better to parallelize over both spatial and channels. Block: (32, 8, 1) = 256 threads. - x: spatial position within tile (up to 32 positions) - y: output channel within tile (up to 8 channels) Grid: (ceil(C_out/8), ceil(HW/32), B) Each thread computes out[b, c_out_block + tid_y, hw_block + tid_x] if in bounds. For 3x3 conv with padding=1: ```cuda float sum = bias[global_c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int in_h = h + dy; int in_w = w + dx; if (in_h >= 0 && in_h < H && in_w >= 0 && in_w < W) { float in_val = in[((b * C_in + c_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w]; float w_val = weight[((global_c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1)]; sum += in_val * w_val; } } } } out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); ``` This is simple but has issues: 1. Memory access for weight is not coalesced if we loop over c_in and dy,dx. Different threads in warp have different global_c_out, so weight accesses are strided by C_in*9. 2. Memory access for input: different threads have different h,w, same b,c_in. For a fixed c_in, adjacent threads (same warp) have adjacent w or h. If they have adjacent w for same h, access is contiguous. Good. Let me redesign weight access. If I tile by C_in, and load filter weights for the current C_in tile into shared memory: ```cuda __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * 9]; // 8 * 9 = 72 floats, tiny ``` Then each thread just reads from shared memory. For input, since different threads read different spatial positions, and we're looping over c_in, we can load a tile of input into shared memory. But the input tile size depends on the spatial tile size and the kernel size. For 3x3 conv with output tile 16x16: input tile is 18x18. With C_inTile=32 channels: 18*18*32 = 10,368 floats = 41,472 bytes. Reasonable! Let me design a proper tiled kernel: ```cuda // Tiled 3x3 conv + ReLU // Block: (16, 16, 1) -> 256 threads for spatial tiling // But then how do we handle output channels? // Alternative: block: (32, 8, 1), each handles 32 spatial pos and 8 output channels // Input tile for 32 spatial pos (e.g., 8x4 rectangle) with 3x3 padding -> 10x6 or similar // That's weird. // Better: process spatial as 1D. 32 contiguous spatial positions. // For H*W spatial positions, map to linear indices. // For 32 adjacent linear positions, the corresponding input region spans // roughly 32+8 = 40 positions (in 1D), but with 2D it's more complex. // Alternative: make spatial tile = 4x4 = 16 positions. Block has 16 threads for spatial, // 16 threads for channels (or use 16x16=256 with 16 spatial and 16 output channels? No, 16 output channels per block is too few). // Actually for small C_out (like 32), we might want to process all output channels in one block. // For large C_out (like 128, 256), we process in chunks of 8 or 16. // Let's use a hybrid: thread block is 2D: (spatial, c_out). // spatial dimension = 16 or 32 // c_out dimension = 8 or 16 // Total = 128 or 256 threads. // For input, load a spatial tile + padding. If spatial tile is 8x4 = 32 positions, // input tile is 10x6. With C_in=32, that's 10*6*32 = 1920 floats. Small! // For C_in=192, process in tiles of 32. __global__ void conv3x3_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE_X = 8; const int SPATIAL_TILE_Y = 4; const int SPATIAL_TILE = SPATIAL_TILE_X * SPATIAL_TILE_Y; // 32 const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw = H * W; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; // 0..31 int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; // 0..7 int local_h = local_hw / SPATIAL_TILE_X; // 0..3 int local_w = local_hw % SPATIAL_TILE_X; // 0..7 int out_h_base = (hw_block / W); int out_w_base = (hw_block % W); // Hmm, linear hw_block doesn't map nicely to 2D with wraparound... ``` This approach is getting complicated because linear spatial indexing doesn't preserve 2D locality well when wrapping around row boundaries. For 3x3 conv, I think a simpler per-thread direct conv is actually acceptable for the feature map sizes in this model, especially if we keep filter weights in shared memory. Let me try a simpler kernel where each thread computes one output element, and we rely on L2 cache for the input. ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_relu_simple( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int hw = H * W; int total = B * C_out * hw; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int tmp = idx / W; int h = tmp % H; tmp = tmp / H; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { int w_base = ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + 0) * 3 + 0; int in_base = ((b * C_in + c_in) * H + 0) * W + 0; #pragma unroll for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { int in_h = h + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int in_w = w + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W) continue; int w_idx = ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1); int in_idx = ((b * C_in + c_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; sum += in[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } out[idx] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This is very simple but has some issues: 1. One thread per output element, no shared memory for filters 2. Weight access pattern: for fixed c_in, dy, dx, threads with different c_out access weight[c_out][c_in][dy][dx]. These are strided by C_in*9. Not coalesced. 3. But for modern GPUs with cache, and small filter sizes, this might be OK. We can improve weight caching by having threads in a block process the same c_out and different spatial positions, or same spatial and different c_out. Actually, if we group by c_out: - A block of 256 threads all compute the same c_out but different (b, h, w). - Then weight access is uniform (broadcast). Good! - But we can only process 256 pixels per block, so many blocks needed. - Grid would be something like (C_out, B * hw / 256). This is actually very similar to the 1x1 conv kernel I designed earlier, but for 3x3. Let me adapt it. For 3x3 conv with block processing same c_out: - Block: (32, 8, 1) or (256, 1, 1) - Each block handles one c_out (or a small group) - Threads compute different (b, h, w) - We can load the filter for the current c_out into shared memory: 9 * C_in floats. For C_in=192, that's 1728 floats = 6.9 KB. For C_in=512, 18.4 KB. Reasonable. - Or into registers if we loop over c_in. If we process 8 output channels per block (like in the 1x1 kernel): - Need 8 * 9 * C_in floats for weights. For C_in=192: 13,824 floats = 55.3 KB. Also reasonable! - Shared memory limit is 227 KB. So we're well within limits. Wait, but then different threads in the warp access different filters. Let me check if that's OK. If block has 8 c_out channels and 32 spatial positions: - Warp 0: c_out[0], spatial[0..31] - Warp 1: c_out[1], spatial[0..31] - etc. - When reading weights for c_in=0, warp 0 reads weight[c_out[0]][c_in][0..8]. Warp 1 reads weight[c_out[1]][c_in][0..8]. These are contiguous in memory if c_out[0] and c_out[1] are adjacent! Yes, weight is stored as [C_out, C_in, 3, 3]. So weight[c_out0*C_in*9 + c_in*9 + k] and weight[c_out1*C_in*9 + c_in*9 + k] are adjacent. With 8 warps reading 9 floats each, that's potentially coalesced if we arrange properly. But actually, if we load weights into shared memory collaboratively first, it doesn't matter. Let me design a 3x3 kernel similar to the 1x1 kernel: ```cuda #define SPATIAL_TILE_3x3 32 #define C_OUT_TILE_3x3 8 #define C_IN_TILE_3x3 32 __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE_3x3; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE_3x3; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_in[(SPATIAL_TILE_3x3 + 8) * (C_IN_TILE_3x3 + 1)]; // pad for 3x3 __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE_3x3 * 9 * C_IN_TILE_3x3]; // 8*9*32 = 2304 floats float sum = bias[global_c_out]; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE_3x3) { // Load input tile with halo for 3x3 // This is tricky because spatial positions are linearized, and the 2D neighborhood // for linearized positions doesn't map to contiguous memory easily. // Maybe just load the center spatial positions and let each thread compute // the 3x3 neighborhood by reading directly from global memory. } } ``` Hmm, loading a 2D spatial tile with halo when positions are linearized is messy. The linear index hw wraps around at W. So spatial positions hw, hw+1 might be on the same row or different rows. The halo of hw includes hw-W-1, hw-W, hw-W+1, hw-1, hw, hw+1, hw+W-1, hw+W, hw+W+1 (with bounds checking). These are not contiguous in memory. For the 3x3 kernel, it might be better to let each thread read directly from global memory. Since the memory is cached, and different threads in a warp might have some locality, it could be OK. But we CAN improve weight access by loading weights into shared memory. Let me do that. For weights of 3x3 conv: [C_out, C_in, 3, 3]. For a block with 8 output channels and C_in_TILE=32, we need 8*32*9 = 2304 floats = 9.2 KB. Very manageable. For input, since each thread reads a 3x3 neighborhood and threads in the warp compute adjacent spatial positions, there is good L2 locality. Let me just read from global memory. Kernel: ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE * 9]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load weights for this C_IN_TILE int tid = local_hw + local_c_out * blockDim.x; int total_weight_elems = C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE * 9; for (int i = tid; i < total_weight_elems; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int rem = i; int w_k = rem % 9; rem /= 9; int w_c_in = rem % C_IN_TILE; rem /= C_IN_TILE; int w_c_out = rem; int global_w_c_in = c_in_base + w_c_in; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; if (global_w_c_in < C_in && global_w_c_out < C_out) { int w_idx = ((global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in) * 3 + (w_k / 3)) * 3 + (w_k % 3); s_weight[i] = weight[w_idx]; } else { s_weight[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute for this C_IN_TILE for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE && c_in_base + c_in_local < C_in; c_in_local++) { // Read 3x3 neighborhood from global memory #pragma unroll for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { int in_h = h + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int in_w = w + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W) continue; int in_idx = ((b * C_in + c_in_base + c_in_local) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; int w_idx = ((local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local) * 3 + (dy + 1)) * 3 + (dx + 1); sum += in[in_idx] * s_weight[w_idx]; } } } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` Wait, this is wrong. For the weight memory layout, I assumed [C_out, C_in, 3, 3] with row-major: weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw] That matches what PyTorch uses for conv2d weights, right? In PyTorch, conv2d weight is [C_out, C_in, kH, kW]. Actually, let me double check. The kernel launch from PyTorch has weight shape [C_out, C_in // groups, kH, kW]. For groups=1, it's [C_out, C_in, kH, kW]. Yes. So weight indexing is correct. For the shared memory weight indexing: s_weight is indexed as [local_c_out][c_in_local][3][3]. For local_c_out in [0, 8), c_in_local in [0, 32), kh in [0,3), kw in [0,3). Linear index = ((local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw. Yes, this matches. For global memory weight indexing: global_w_idx = ((global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw. Yes, correct. One issue: the input reads from global memory are not fully coalesced because threads in the warp with different w (or different h if w wraps) read different addresses. But for threads on the same row with adjacent w, the reads for the same (c_in, dy, dx) are adjacent. Since blockDim.x = 32, if W >= 32 and hw_block starts on a row boundary, then threads 0..31 are all on the same row. Their reads for the same (c_in, dy, dx) are at addresses w, w+1, w+2, ..., w+31. Contiguous! Coalesced! If hw_block is such that some threads wrap to the next row, the adjacency breaks at the boundary. But for most tiles this is fine. This kernel should be reasonably efficient. Now for 5x5 conv, it's similar but with 25 multiplies per c_in instead of 9: ```cuda __global__ void conv5x5_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; // Similar to 3x3 but with 5x5 neighborhood and pad=2 } ``` For 5x5, memory traffic per thread is higher (25*C_in vs 9*C_in). But 5x5 convs are less common in the model. Let me check the inception module architecture again: - 3a: branch5x5: 192 -> 16 -> 32. Very small. - 3b: branch5x5: 256 -> 32 -> 96. Still small. - 4a: branch5x5: 480 -> 16 -> 48. Small. - 4b: branch5x5: 512 -> 24 -> 64. Small. - 4c: branch5x5: 512 -> 24 -> 64. Small. - 4d: branch5x5: 512 -> 32 -> 64. Small. - 4e: branch5x5: 528 -> 32 -> 128. Small. - 5a: branch5x5: 832 -> 32 -> 128. Small. - 5b: branch5x5: 832 -> 48 -> 128. Small. So 5x5 branches are never the bottleneck. I can write a simpler kernel for them. **Fused sequential conv (1x1 + relu + kxk + relu):** I mentioned this earlier. Can I fuse these? For 1x1 -> 3x3: both preserve spatial dimensions with padding. For a block processing 8 output channels and 16 spatial positions: - Input: [B, C_in, H, W] - Intermediate after 1x1: [B, C_mid, H, W] - Output after 3x3: [B, C_out, H, W] If I tile by input channels C_in, I can: 1. For each C_in tile: a. Load C_in weights for 1x1 conv (8*C_in_TILE floats) b. Load input spatial tile + halo for 3x3 c. Compute 1x1 conv for the spatial tile, producing intermediate d. Load 3x3 weights for this C_in tile? No, 3x3 weights are [C_out, C_mid, 3, 3], not indexed by C_in. Wait, that's the problem. In a sequential conv 1x1 (C_in -> C_mid) followed by 3x3 (C_mid -> C_out), the output of the first is the input of the second. The 3x3 conv doesn't use C_in. To fuse them, I need to process in tiles of C_mid, not C_in: 1. For each C_mid tile: a. Compute 1x1 conv from all C_in to this C_mid tile b. Apply ReLU c. Compute 3x3 conv from this C_mid tile to all C_out, accumulate For step (a), each thread needs to compute the 1x1 conv for its spatial position and the current C_mid. This requires loading all C_in for the spatial position, and the 1x1 weights for the C_mid. For step (c), each thread accumulates the 3x3 contribution from the current C_mid tile. This requires loading the 3x3 weights. This is getting very complex. Given time constraints, I'll write separate kernels for each operation and rely on avoiding intermediate allocations rather than full kernel fusion. Actually, for sequential convs like reduce_3x3 -> conv_3x3, I can allocate the intermediate buffer once and reuse it. The memory bandwidth is the main cost. Hmm, but having separate kernels means writing intermediate to global memory and reading it back. That's a lot of bandwidth. For the reduce_3x3 branch: - 1x1 conv from C_in to C_mid: reads C_in, writes C_mid - ReLU - 3x3 conv from C_mid to C_out: reads C_mid, writes C_out If I can fuse these into one kernel, I save writing/reading C_mid. Given that this is probably one of the main optimization opportunities, let me try to write a fused 1x1 -> 3x3 kernel. **Fused 1x1_3x3 kernel design:** Input: [B, C_in, H, W] Output: [B, C_out, H, W] 1x1 weights: [C_mid, C_in] 3x3 weights: [C_out, C_mid, 3, 3] Approach: tile over C_mid. For each output tile (8 c_out channels, 16 spatial positions): Initialize accumulators to bias[c_out] For each C_mid tile (e.g., 8 or 16 channels): a. Load 3x3 weights for this C_mid tile and the 8 c_out channels: 8 * C_midTile * 9 floats b. For each spatial position in the tile: i. To compute 3x3 for the current C_mid, we need the intermediate 1x1 output for the C_mid tile across the 3x3 neighborhood. ii. The 1x1 output for a spatial position and C_mid channel is: dot(in[b, :, h, w], weight_1x1[C_mid, :]). iii. We can compute this on the fly by loading the input and 1x1 weights. For step b.ii, if we compute the 1x1 output on the fly for each of the 9 spatial positions needed by 3x3, that's 9 * dot(C_in) per output pixel per C_mid. Very expensive because we're computing the same 1x1 outputs multiple times. Better: pre-compute the 1x1 outputs for the entire C_mid tile and store in shared memory or registers. For a spatial tile of 16 positions and C_mid tile of 16 channels: - Intermediate buffer: 16 * 16 = 256 floats per block. Small! - But the 3x3 conv needs a 18x18 region for a 16x16 output tile (with padding). That's 324 * 16 = 5184 floats. Still manageable (20.7 KB). Wait, with a 16x16 spatial tile, the intermediate needs to be 18x18 (for 3x3 with pad=1). And for C_midTile=16, that's 18*18*16 = 5184 floats. Then we compute 3x3 from this intermediate to 8 output channels. The 3x3 weights need 8 * 16 * 9 = 1152 floats. So total shared memory: 5184 + 1152 = 6336 floats = 25.3 KB. Fine! Additionally, to compute the 1x1 intermediate, we need to load input and 1x1 weights. For input 18x18 and C_in=96, that's 18*18*96 = 31,104 floats per C_in tile... wait, but we tile on C_in too. Actually, the 1x1 conv is over all C_in, producing C_mid. We can also tile on C_in: For each C_in tile (say 32 channels): 1. Load input: 18x18x32 = 10,368 floats 2. Load 1x1 weights: C_midTile * 32 = 16*32 = 512 floats 3. Compute partial 1x1 and accumulate in intermediate shared memory After processing all C_in tiles, intermediate is complete. Then compute 3x3. Total shared memory per iteration: - input: 18*18*32 = 10,368 floats = 41.5 KB - intermediate (accumulate across C_in tiles): 18*18*16 = 5184 floats = 20.7 KB - 1x1 weights: 512 floats = 2 KB - 3x3 weights: 8*16*9 = 1152 floats = 4.6 KB Total: ~69 KB. Acceptable but getting close to shared memory limit for some cases. For C_in=192 and C_midTile=32: - input: 18*18*32 = 41.5 KB - intermediate: 18*18*32 = 41.5 KB - Total just these two: 83 KB. Plus weights. Getting large. For larger C_in, we'd need smaller C_midTile or smaller spatial tile. Alternatively, instead of storing the full intermediate spatial+C_mid tile, we can process the 3x3 conv on the fly while computing the 1x1. But as I noted, this requires recomputing 1x1 outputs for the 3x3 neighborhood. For a 3x3 neighborhood, each output pixel needs 9 intermediate values. If we compute each intermediate value by dotting C_in inputs, that's 9*C_in multiplies. For the on-the-fly approach, each thread computes one output pixel, looping over C_in and accumulating both the 1x1 and 3x3 together. Actually! Here's the insight: For output pixel (h, w), 3x3 conv does: out[h,w] = sum_{dy,dx} conv3x3_weight[dy,dx,c_mid] * mid[h+dy, w+dx, c_mid] And mid[h+dy, w+dx, c_mid] = sum_{c_in} conv1x1_weight[c_mid, c_in] * in[h+dy, w+dx, c_in] So: out[h,w] = sum_{dy,dx,c_mid,c_in} conv3x3_weight[dy,dx,c_mid] * conv1x1_weight[c_mid, c_in] * in[h+dy, w+dx, c_in] This is exactly a 3x3 conv where the effective weight is: effective_weight[dy,dx,c_in] = sum_{c_mid} conv3x3_weight[dy,dx,c_mid] * conv1x1_weight[c_mid, c_in] We can precompute this once per layer! It's a matrix multiplication of [9, C_mid] by [C_mid, C_in] = [9, C_in]. Then the fused operation is just one 3x3 conv with these precomputed weights! Wait, but the first conv has its own bias, and we need ReLU between them. mid[h,w,c_mid] = relu(sum_{c_in} w1x1[c_mid, c_in] * in[h,w,c_in] + b1[c_mid]) Then: out[h,w,c_out] = relu(sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * mid[h+dy, w+dx, c_mid] + b3[c_out]) Because of the non-linearity (ReLU) in the middle, we cannot precompute the effective weights. The two convs don't compose linearly. So on-the-fly computation or precomputing intermediate are the only options. Given the complexity of fully fusing with shared memory, let me fall back to a simpler approach: - For 1x1 -> 3x3 and 1x1 -> 5x5, just call two separate kernels but reuse a persistent intermediate buffer. - The intermediate buffer is allocated once and reused for all sequential convs. This isn't as fast as full fusion, but it's much simpler and avoids allocation overhead. For pool + 1x1 conv, I CAN fully fuse because maxpool -> 1x1 is just: out = relu(sum_{c_in} w[c_out, c_in] * maxpool3x3(in[:, c_in, h, w]) + b[c_out]) The maxpool is over a 3x3 neighborhood (with stride 1, pad 1), and the 1x1 is just a dot product. For each output pixel, I can compute the maxpool on the fly for each c_in, then do the 1x1 conv. No intermediate buffer needed! **Fused maxpool3x3_stride1 + 1x1 + ReLU kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void maxpool3x3_1x1_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load weights int tid = local_hw + local_c_out * blockDim.x; for (int i = tid; i < C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE; i += blockDim.x * blockDim.y) { int w_c_out = i / C_IN_TILE; int w_c_in = i % C_IN_TILE; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; int global_w_c_in = c_in_base + w_c_in; if (global_w_c_out < C_out && global_w_c_in < C_in) { s_weight[i] = weight[global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in]; } else { s_weight[i] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE && c_in_base + c_in_local < C_in; c_in_local++) { // Compute 3x3 maxpool float max_val = -1e20f; #pragma unroll for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { int in_h = h + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int in_w = w + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W) continue; int in_addr = ((b * C_in + c_in_base + c_in_local) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; float val = in[in_addr]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } sum += max_val * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]; } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This is nice and compact! **Initial layers:** For conv1 (7x7, stride=2, pad=3) and maxpool1 (3x3, stride=2, pad=1). I'll write a separate kernel for conv1 + relu. Actually, since it's a one-time large conv, I'll write a simple kernel. ```cuda __global__ void conv7x7_stride2_relu( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { int hw = H_out * W_out; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * hw; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; int in_h_base = h * 2 - 3; int in_w_base = w * 2 - 3; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { for (int dy = 0; dy < 7; dy++) { int in_h = in_h_base + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H_in) continue; for (int dx = 0; dx < 7; dx++) { int in_w = in_w_base + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W_in) continue; int in_idx = ((b * C_in + c_in) * H_in + in_h) * W_in + in_w; int w_idx = ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 7 + dy) * 7 + dx; sum += in[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } out[idx] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } ``` This is simple and correct. With C_in=3, it's not too expensive per thread. Then maxpool1 (3x3, stride=2, pad=1) on 112x112 -> 56x56: ```cuda __global__ void maxpool3x3_stride2( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, int B, int C, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { int hw = H_out * W_out; int total = B * C * hw; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp % C; int b = tmp / C; int in_h_base = h * 2 - 1; int in_w_base = w * 2 - 1; float max_val = -1e20f; for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; dy++) { int in_h = in_h_base + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H_in) continue; for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; dx++) { int in_w = in_w_base + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W_in) continue; int in_idx = ((b * C + c) * H_in + in_h) * W_in + in_w; float val = in[in_idx]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } out[idx] = max_val; } ``` For conv3 (3x3, pad=1) on 56x56: Similar to the conv3x3_relu kernel but with H=W=56. For maxpool2, maxpool3, maxpool4: Same as maxpool3x3_stride2 but on different sizes. For the avgpool at the end: Input is 7x7 or similar, output is 1x1. AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1)) means global average pooling. ```cuda __global__ void global_avgpool( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, int B, int C, int H, int W ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C; if (idx >= total) return; int c = idx % C; int b = idx / C; float sum = 0; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { for (int w = 0; w < W; w++) { sum += in[((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w]; } } out[idx] = sum / (H * W); } ``` Then flatten is just a memory layout change (or no-op if we use it directly). FC: matrix multiplication of [B, 1024] by [1024, 1000]. I can write a simple GEMM kernel, or use PyTorch for this since it's just one operation and cuDNN will do well. Actually, for the FC layer, I can write a simple kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fc_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out; if (idx >= total) return; int c_out = idx % C_out; int b = idx / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { sum += in[b * C_in + c_in] * weight[c_out * C_in + c_in]; } out[idx] = sum; } ``` Wait, PyTorch linear layer computes `x @ W.T + b`, where W is [C_out, C_in] and x is [B, C_in]. Yes, the above is correct. Dropout with p=0.0 is a no-op. Now let me think about buffer management. In the forward pass, I need temporary buffers between layers. I'll allocate a few reusable buffers in __init__ and reuse them. But since we're writing a CUDA extension, we can allocate tensors in Python using torch.empty() and pass them to the kernels. In the Inception modules, the output of each branch needs to go into a region of the concatenated output buffer. I'll pre-allocate one output buffer per inception module (or reuse buffers). Let me count the memory requirements for activations at each layer: After conv1+maxpool1: 10*64*56*56 = 1,996,800 floats = 8 MB After conv2: 10*64*56*56 = 8 MB After conv3+maxpool2: 10*192*28*28 = 1,505,280 floats = 6 MB After inception3a: 10*256*28*28 = 2,007,040 floats = 8 MB After inception3b: 10*480*28*28 = 3,763,200 floats = 15.1 MB After maxpool3: 10*480*14*14 = 940,800 floats = 3.8 MB After inception4a: 10*512*14*14 = 1,003,520 floats = 4 MB After 4b, 4c, 4d, 4e: same size, 4 MB each After maxpool4: 10*528*7*7 = 258,720 floats = 1 MB After 5a, 5b: 10*832*7*7 = 407,680 floats = 1.6 MB After avgpool: 10*1024*1*1 = 10,240 floats = 40 KB So the largest activation is ~15 MB. I can reuse 2-3 buffers and be fine. Actually, since each layer's output is the next layer's input, I really just need ping-pong between two buffers for most operations. But for inception modules with 4 branches writing to the same output, I need one output buffer. Let me design the Python code structure: In `ModelNew.__init__`: 1. Copy all weights from the reference model (or create and initialize them) 2. Pre-allocate reusable buffers Actually, looking at the test code, the model is instantiated via `ModelNew(*get_init_inputs())`. The weights might be randomly initialized. But for correctness checking, the evaluator will compare outputs given the same inputs. Wait, the problem says "Replace the forward computation". So ModelNew must have the same weights as the reference. But how are the weights initialized? In the test code, they create `Model(num_classes)` and `ModelNew(num_classes)`. The weights are randomly initialized (PyTorch default). For correctness comparing, the evaluator probably: 1. Creates Model and ModelNew with same init args 2. Copies weights from Model to ModelNew, OR evaluates both with random weights and compares Actually, looking at typical evaluations, they usually instantiate both models, and since they use the same random seed or just compare outputs for the same architecture with default initialization... For maxpool operations (stride=2): ```cuda __global__ void maxpool3x3_stride2_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, int B, int C, int H_in, int W_in ) { int H_out = (H_in + 2*1 - 3) / 2 + 1; int W_out = (W_in + 2*1 - 3) / 2 + 1; int hw_out = H_out * W_out; int total = B * C * hw_out; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; int tmp = idx / W_out; int h = tmp % H_out; tmp = tmp / H_out; int c = tmp % C; int b = tmp / C; int in_h_base = h * 2 - 1; int in_w_base = w * 2 - 1; float max_val = -1e20f; for (int dy = 0; dy < 3; dy++) { int in_h = in_h_base + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H_in) continue; for (int dx = 0; dx < 3; dx++) { int in_w = in_w_base + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W_in) continue; float val = in[((b * C + c) * H_in + in_h) * W_in + in_w]; if (val > max_val) max_val = val; } } out[((b * C + c) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = max_val; } ``` I should compute H_out and W_out in the host code and pass them, rather than computing in kernel. But computing in kernel is fine. Actually, to simplify, let me compute output sizes in Python and pass them. For `global_avgpool_kernel`: ```cuda __global__ void global_avgpool_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, int B, int C, int H, int W ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C; if (idx >= total) return; int c = idx % C; int b = idx / C; float sum = 0.0f; for (int h = 0; h < H; h++) { int base = ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W; for (int w = 0; w < W; w++) { sum += in[base + w]; } } out[idx] = sum / (H * W); } ``` For `fc_kernel`: ```cuda __global__ void fc_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out; if (idx >= total) return; int c_out = idx % C_out; int b = idx / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; int in_base = b * C_in; int w_base = c_out * C_in; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { sum += in[in_base + c_in] * weight[w_base + c_in]; } out[idx] = sum; } ``` For the `dropout` with p=0.0, it's a no-op, so we skip it. In the reference: - branch1x1: conv, no explicit relu in the branch (but wait, looking at the code...)
