But in the Model.forward, there are ReLUs: ```python x = self.maxpool1(F.relu(self.conv1(x))) x = F.relu(self.conv2(x)) x = self.maxpool2(F.relu(self.conv3(x))) x = self.inception3a(x) # No relu here! x = self.inception3b(x) # ... x = self.inception5b(x) x = self.avgpool(x) x = torch.flatten(x, 1) x = self.dropout(x) x = self.fc(x) ``` So in the reference, ReLU is only applied after conv1, conv2, conv3, and never inside or after inception modules! This is VERY important. I don't need ReLU in the inception branches! This simplifies things significantly. Wait, let me double check the reference implementation... Looking at actual GoogLeNet architecture, there typically ARE ReLUs after each conv. But the reference code as provided doesn't include them. I must match the reference exactly. So for my kernels: - conv1 + relu + maxpool1 - conv2 + relu - conv3 + relu + maxpool2 - inception3a: NO relu inside branches - inception3b: NO relu inside branches - maxpool3 - inception4a-e: NO relu - maxpool4 - inception5a-b: NO relu - avgpool + flatten + dropout + fc This means I can write simpler inception branch kernels without ReLU. I can also use PyTorch conv2d for the inception branches and still be correct, since the main overhead might be elsewhere. But to get speedup, I should still write custom kernels for the inception branches to avoid allocation overhead. Wait, actually if there are no ReLUs in the branches, the fusion opportunities are somewhat reduced. But we can still: - Avoid intermediate allocations for branch outputs by writing directly to concatenated buffer - Avoid concat operation overhead Actually, for each inception branch, PyTorch's conv2d will produce a tensor, and then we concat them. With 4 branches per module and 9 modules, that's 36 temporary tensors plus 9 concat operations per forward pass. If I write custom kernels that write directly to slices of the output tensor, I save: 1. 36 temporary allocations 2. 9 concat operations (which are just copies in PyTorch) The concat in PyTorch for CUDA is actually a single kernel launch, but it does involve memory copies. Writing directly to the output avoids this. For the branches without ReLU, my kernels are similar but without the `fmaxf(sum, 0.0f)`. Actually, let me reconsider whether I need to write all these custom kernels, or whether I can use PyTorch's conv2d for the branches and just optimize the concat. PyTorch's conv2d + concat on CUDA probably does: 1. Launch cuDNN conv for branch0 2. Launch cuDNN conv for branch1 step 1 3. Launch cuDNN conv for branch1 step 2 4. Repeat for branch2, branch3 5. Launch concat kernel That's about 7 kernel launches per inception module. My custom approach can reduce this to 4 kernel launches (one per branch), and the output is already concatenated (no concat kernel needed). Plus, if I don't need ReLU, the branch kernels are simpler. The real question is whether my custom conv kernels can beat cuDNN for these specific sizes. cuDNN is very optimized, but for small convs and high channel counts, my tiled kernels might be competitive. For branches with 1x1 convs, my kernel is specifically optimized for 1x1, which is essentially GEMM. For small feature maps (7x7, 14x14) and high channels, I might actually do well because I'm avoiding some cuDNN overhead. For branches with 3x3 and 5x5 convs, especially when preceded by 1x1, there are two cuDNN calls vs one custom kernel. Even if my custom 3x3/5x5 is slightly slower than cuDNN, avoiding the intermediate write/read might still win. Let me quantify the memory savings for a typical branch (e.g., branch1 in inception3a): - 1x1 conv: 192 -> 96 at 28x28. Reads input: 10*192*28*28=1.5M floats. Writes intermediate: 10*96*28*28=0.75M floats. - 3x3 conv: 96 -> 128 at 28x28. Reads intermediate: 0.75M. Writes output: 10*128*28*28=1M floats. - Total memory with PyTorch: ~4.25M floats read/written. - With fused custom kernel: reads input 1.5M, writes output 1M. Total 2.5M floats. - Savings: ~40% memory bandwidth plus no intermediate allocation. This is significant! Let me proceed with writing the fused kernels. But wait, I said I couldn't easily fuse 1x1 -> 3x3 due to the ReLU in between. But there's no ReLU! So I CAN fuse them linearly! For a sequence conv1x1 + conv3x3 without activation in between: out = conv3x3(conv1x1(in)) Since both are linear operations (conv is just a special case of linear), we can compose them! Specifically: 1x1 conv: for each spatial position (h,w), mid[h,w,c_mid] = sum_{c_in} w1x1[c_mid, c_in] * in[h,w,c_in] + b1[c_mid] 3x3 conv: out[h,w,c_out] = sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * mid[h+dy, w+dx, c_mid] + b3[c_out] Substituting: out[h,w,c_out] = sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * (sum_{c_in} w1x1[c_mid, c_in] * in[h+dy, w+dx, c_in] + b1[c_mid]) + b3[c_out] = sum_{dy,dx,c_in} (sum_{c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * w1x1[c_mid, c_in]) * in[h+dy, w+dx, c_in] + sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * b1[c_mid] + b3[c_out] The effective weight is: w_eff[c_out, c_in, dy, dx] = sum_{c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * w1x1[c_mid, c_in] And the effective bias is: b_eff[c_out] = b3[c_out] + sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3x3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * b1[c_mid] Since there are no ReLUs between the two convs, this is exact! This is AMAZING! I can precompute the effective weights and biases for each sequential branch, then just use a single 3x3 conv kernel (or 5x5) for the entire branch! Similarly for branch2 (1x1 -> 5x5). For branch3 (pool -> 1x1), pool is NOT linear, so I can't fuse it with 1x1. But I already wrote a fused maxpool+1x1 kernel. For branch0 (1x1), it's already a single conv. So the inception module reduces to: - Branch0: 1x1 conv - Branch1: effective 3x3 conv with precomputed weights - Branch2: effective 5x5 conv with precomputed weights - Branch3: fused maxpool + 1x1 conv This is a huge simplification and performance win! Let me verify for branch1: - W1 shape: [C_mid, C_in] - W3 shape: [C_out, C_mid, 3, 3] - W_eff shape: [C_out, C_in, 3, 3] Computation: For each c_out, c_in, dy, dx: w_eff[c_out, c_in, dy, dx] = sum_{c_mid=0}^{C_mid-1} w3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * w1[c_mid, c_in] In Python (using PyTorch): ```python w1 = self.inception3a.branch3x3[0].weight # [C_mid, C_in, 1, 1] b1 = self.inception3a.branch3x3[0].bias # [C_mid] w3 = self.inception3a.branch3x3[1].weight # [C_out, C_mid, 3, 3] b3 = self.inception3a.branch3x3[1].bias # [C_out] # Compute effective weight # w1 is [C_mid, C_in, 1, 1], squeeze to [C_mid, C_in] w1_squeezed = w1.squeeze(-1).squeeze(-1) # [C_mid, C_in] w3_reshaped = w3.view(C_out, C_mid, 9) # [C_out, C_mid, 9] # Effective weight: [C_out, C_mid, 9] @ [C_mid, C_in] = [C_out, C_in, 9] w_eff = torch.matmul(w3_reshaped, w1_squeezed) # [C_out, C_in, 9] w_eff = w_eff.view(C_out, C_in, 3, 3) # Effective bias # b_eff[c_out] = b3[c_out] + sum_{dy,dx,c_mid} w3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * b1[c_mid] b_eff = b3 + (w3.sum(dim=[2,3]) * b1.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=1) ``` Wait, let me double check the bias computation: b_eff[c_out] = b3[c_out] + sum_{dy,dx} sum_{c_mid} w3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * b1[c_mid] sum_{dy,dx} w3[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] is just w3.sum(dim=[2,3])[c_out, c_mid]. So: temp = (w3.sum(dim=[2,3]) * b1).sum(dim=1) # shape [C_out] b_eff = b3 + temp Yes. For 5x5: w_eff[c_out, c_in, dy, dx] = sum_{c_mid} w5[c_out, c_mid, dy, dx] * w1[c_mid, c_in] b_eff = b5 + (w5.sum(dim=[2,3]) * b1).sum(dim=1) This works because there's no activation between the convs. For inception modules, I can precompute all these effective weights in __init__ and store them as buffers. But wait, I need to be careful. The sequential convs in the reference are: ```python self.branch3x3 = nn.Sequential( nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_3x3, kernel_size=1), nn.Conv2d(reduce_3x3, out_3x3, kernel_size=3, padding=1) ) ``` There's no ReLU between them. So yes, they compose linearly. This is a huge simplification! Let me redesign my approach: **ModelNew.__init__:** 1. Create all the individual conv layers as before (or create nn.Sequential modules like the reference) 2. For each sequential branch (branch3x3, branch5x5 in each inception module): a. Precompute effective weights and biases b. Store as CUDA tensors 3. For single conv branches and pool branches, keep the original weights **ModelNew.forward:** 1. Early layers (conv1, maxpool1, conv2, conv3, maxpool2) with simple kernels 2. For each inception module: a. Allocate output tensor for concatenated result b. Launch branch0 kernel (1x1 conv) c. Launch branch1 kernel (effective 3x3 conv) d. Launch branch2 kernel (effective 5x5 conv) e. Launch branch3 kernel (fused maxpool+1x1) 3. Maxpool3, maxpool4 as needed 4. Global avgpool, FC This means I only need 1 kernel per branch instead of up to 3! Now let me think about the early layers again: - conv1: 3->64, 7x7, stride=2, pad=3 - maxpool1: 3x3, stride=2, pad=1 - conv2: 64->64, 1x1 - conv3: 64->192, 3x3, pad=1 - maxpool2: 3x3, stride=2, pad=1 Since conv1 and maxpool1 can't be easily fused without redundant computation, I'll keep them separate but use custom kernels. conv2 is 1x1, use my optimized kernel. conv3 is 3x3, use my optimized kernel. maxpool2 is straightforward. For the 1x1 conv kernel without ReLU (since conv2 has ReLU but the inception 1x1 branches don't), I'll write a version with and without ReLU. Or I can add a boolean parameter. Actually, I'll just write two versions: `conv1x1_relu` and `conv1x1`. Same for `conv3x3_relu` and `conv3x3`, `conv5x5_relu` and `conv5x5`, `conv7x7_relu` and `conv7x7`. Let me re-read: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def forward(self, x): x = self.maxpool1(F.relu(self.conv1(x))) x = F.relu(self.conv2(x)) x = self.maxpool2(F.relu(self.conv3(x))) x = self.inception3a(x) x = self.inception3b(x) x = self.maxpool3(x) x = self.inception4a(x) x = self.inception4b(x) x = self.inception4c(x) x = self.inception4d(x) x = self.inception4e(x) x = self.maxpool4(x) x = self.inception5a(x) x = self.inception5b(x) x = self.avgpool(x) x = torch.flatten(x, 1) x = self.dropout(x) x = self.fc(x) return x ``` And: ```python class InceptionModule(nn.Module): def forward(self, x): branch1x1 = self.branch1x1(x) branch3x3 = self.branch3x3(x) branch5x5 = self.branch5x5(x) branch_pool = self.branch_pool(x) outputs = [branch1x1, branch3x3, branch5x5, branch_pool] return torch.cat(outputs, 1) ``` So yes: - conv1 has ReLU before maxpool1 - conv2 has ReLU (but no maxpool after it!) - conv3 has ReLU before maxpool2 - NO ReLU in inception modules at all - NO ReLU after maxpool3, maxpool4 - NO ReLU after inception5b - avgpool, flatten, dropout (p=0.0), fc My kernels need to match this exactly. Now, for the effective weight precomputation in __init__, I'll need to do it after the modules are created. Let me write a helper. For the maxpool operations: maxpool1, maxpool2, maxpool3, maxpool4 all use 3x3, stride=2, padding=1. The output size formula is: H_out = floor((H_in + 2*padding - kernel_size) / stride) + 1 = floor((H_in + 2 - 3) / 2) + 1 = floor((H_in - 1) / 2) + 1 For H_in = 224: floor(223/2)+1 = 111+1 = 112 ✓ For H_in = 112: floor(111/2)+1 = 55+1 = 56 ✓ For H_in = 56: floor(55/2)+1 = 27+1 = 28 ✓ For H_in = 28: floor(27/2)+1 = 13+1 = 14 ✓ For H_in = 14: floor(13/2)+1 = 6+1 = 7 ✓ AdaptiveAvgPool2d((1,1)) on 7x7 gives 1x1. For conv1: (224 + 2*3 - 7)/2 + 1 = (224 + 6 - 7)/2 + 1 = 223/2 + 1 = 111 + 1 = 112 ✓ Great. Now let me design the complete kernel file. I'll write: 1. CUDA source with all kernels 2. C++ wrapper functions 3. Python ModelNew class that loads the inline extension For the C++ wrappers, `load_inline` needs host functions that call the kernels. Here's an example: ```cpp void conv1x1_relu_cuda(torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor in, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { dim3 block(32, 8); dim3 grid((C_out + 7) / 8, (H * W + 31) / 32, B); conv1x1_relu_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(out.data_ptr<float>(), in.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, C_in, C_out, H, W); } ``` Wait, but `load_inline` with `functions=['conv1x1_relu_cuda', ...]` will auto-generate pybind11 bindings for these functions. Actually, I need to make sure I use the correct Torch C++ API. For PyTorch C++ extensions, the typical signature is: ```cpp torch::Tensor my_function(torch::Tensor input, ...) { auto output = torch::empty(...); kernel<<<...>>>(...); return output; } ``` Or with output passed in: ```cpp void my_function(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor output) { kernel<<<...>>>(...); } ``` Either works with pybind11. The Python side can call these directly. For simplicity, I'll have the C++ functions create and return output tensors, except for branches that write to a slice of a larger tensor. For branches writing to slices, I'll pass the output tensor, along with offset information. Wait, for CUDA kernels, writing to a slice is tricky if we want coalesced memory access, because slices might not start at the beginning of the tensor. But using tensor.data_ptr() + offset works fine. In PyTorch, `tensor[:, start:end]` returns a view, and `.data_ptr()` gives the address of the first element. But passing slices to C++ with pybind11 directly might not work because pybind11 might copy or not handle Tensor views properly. Actually, `torch::Tensor` in pybind11 should handle views correctly. The data_ptr of a sliced tensor will be correct. But there's a subtlety: the stride of the sliced tensor. For a contiguous tensor sliced on dimension 1, the result might not be contiguous. `out[:, 0:64]` on a [B, 256, H, W] tensor is contiguous in memory? Actually, PyTorch tensors sliced on the first dimension are contiguous if the original was. For NCHW, slicing on C gives a tensor with the same stride except along C. The elements within each slice are contiguous, and the next slice starts where the previous ended. So it's contiguous in memory. Let me verify: for [B, 256, H, W], the offset of [b, c, h, w] is ((b * 256 + c) * H + h) * W + w. Slice [:, 0:64] has offset ((b * 256 + c) * H + h) * W + w where c in [0, 63). This is contiguous. And the stride is [256*H*W, H*W, W, 1]. So data_ptr() + offset for [b, 0, h, w] is b*256*H*W + 0 + h*W + w = correct. For `.data_ptr()` of the sliced tensor: it returns the address of element [0, 0, 0, 0], which is correct for writing to the slice. But the kernel shouldn't use strides - it should just use the data_ptr directly, assuming contiguous storage. Since the slice of a contiguous tensor is contiguous, this is fine. However, we need to make sure the output tensor we allocate is contiguous. `torch.empty(...)` creates a contiguous tensor, so slicing it gives contiguous views. Wait, actually `torch.empty(B, 256, 28, 28)` is contiguous. `out[:, 0:64]` is a view that's contiguous in memory? Yes, because the slice covers the first 64 channels continuously. So I can pass `out[:, 0:64].contiguous()` or just `out[:, 0:64]` to the C++ function. But the C++ function receives a `torch::Tensor`, and calling `.data_ptr<float>()` on it will give the correct starting address. One concern: inside the kernel, I'm computing output indices using the full tensor dimensions, not the slice dimensions. For example, if I pass `out_3a[:, 0:64]` to the kernel, the kernel should know it's writing to channels 0-63 of a 256-channel tensor. Actually, the simplest approach is to pass the full output tensor, plus a channel offset, and have the kernel write at the correct offset. Or, the kernel can just receive the sliced tensor, and the 'C_out' parameter should be the slice size (64, not 256). The kernel computes indices within the slice, and the base pointer points to the start of the slice. This works perfectly. So for branch0 writing to channels 0-64: - Pass: `out_3a[:, 0:64]`, C_out=64 - Kernel writes with addresses relative to the slice start. Great. For the C++ wrapper, I can either: 1. Accept all parameters and let Python pass sliced tensors 2. Accept full tensor + offset and compute pointer in C++ I'll go with option 1 because it's simpler. """ cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> ... CUDA kernels here ... ... wrapper functions here ... """ # Actually for load_inline, cuda_source is the .cu file content and cpp_sources is .cpp content # Or we can put everything in cpp_sources and use cuda_sources for just the kernel part. # In load_inline: # cuda_sources=cuda_source, # cpp_sources=cpp_source, # functions=['conv7x7_stride2_relu_cuda', ...] ``` Wait, I need to be careful with `load_inline`. The typical usage is: ```python mod = load_inline( name="my_cuda", cpp_sources=cpp_source_with_wrapper, cuda_sources=cuda_source_with_kernels, functions=["func1", "func2"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], ) ``` The `cpp_sources` should contain the C++ wrapper functions, and `cuda_sources` should contain the CUDA kernels. Or I can put everything in `cpp_sources` with `__global__` functions - but actually kernels need to be in `.cu` compilation unit. Let me check: in `load_inline`, `cpp_sources` is compiled as C++ and `cuda_sources` is compiled as CUDA. Functions in both can call each other. But the kernels (`__global__`) must be in `cuda_sources`. For pybind11 bindings, `load_inline` auto-generates them from the `functions` list. The functions listed must be accessible - typically declared/defined in `cpp_sources`. So the structure should be: - `cuda_sources`: contains the `__global__` kernels - `cpp_sources`: contains C++ wrapper functions that call the kernels, and also includes headers - `functions`: list of wrapper function names But the C++ wrappers need to see the kernel declarations. One way is to put the kernels in `cuda_sources` and forward-declare them in `cpp_sources`. Actually, a simpler approach: put everything in `cuda_sources`, including both kernels and wrappers. Then `cpp_sources` can be empty or omitted. Wait, does `load_inline` allow this? Let me think. The `cuda_sources` is compiled with nvcc, which can handle both host and device code. So yes, I can put the wrapper functions (with pybind11-ready signatures) in `cuda_sources` along with the kernels. Actually, `load_inline` might expect C++ functions to be in `cpp_sources`. Let me use a hybrid approach: - Put kernels in `cuda_sources` - Put wrapper functions in `cpp_sources`, with `extern` declarations for the kernels Hmm, but if the wrappers are in the C++ translation unit and kernels are in the CUDA translation unit, the C++ compiler needs to know about them. Since kernels aren't callable directly from C++, the C++ wrapper would use `<<< >>>` syntax which isn't valid in C++. Actually, `<<< >>>` is CUDA syntax. If the wrappers are in the `.cpp` file compiled by the host compiler, it won't understand `<<< >>>`. So the wrappers MUST be in the `.cu` file (or a `.cpp` file compiled by nvcc). Wait, `load_inline` compiles `cpp_sources` with the C++ compiler and `cuda_sources` with nvcc, then links them together. But `<<< >>>` is only valid in CUDA source files. So the wrappers should be in `cuda_sources`! The `cpp_sources` can contain helper declarations if needed, but typically for simple cases, everything goes in `cuda_sources`. Let me verify by looking at common `load_inline` usage patterns. In many PyTorch extensions, the whole file is passed as `cuda_sources`: ```python cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void kernel(...) { ... } void wrapper(torch::Tensor a, ...) { kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...); } """ mod = load_inline( name="ext", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["wrapper"], ... ) ``` This should work fine because nvcc compiles the `.cu` file and handles both host and device code. Let me use this approach. For the Inception modules, I need to precompute the effective weights. In __init__: ```python # For InceptionModule, after creating the conv layers: def _compute_effective_conv(self, conv1, convk): # conv1: nn.Conv2d(C_in, C_mid, 1x1) # convk: nn.Conv2d(C_mid, C_out, kxk, padding=(k-1)//2) w1 = conv1.weight.squeeze(-1).squeeze(-1) # [C_mid, C_in] wk = convk.weight # [C_out, C_mid, k, k] b1 = conv1.bias # [C_mid] bk = convk.bias # [C_out] C_out, C_mid, kH, kW = wk.shape C_mid, C_in = w1.shape # Reshape for matmul wk_reshaped = wk.view(C_out, C_mid, kH * kW) # [C_out, C_mid, k*k] # matmul: [C_out, C_mid, k*k] @ [C_mid, C_in] -> need [C_out, k*k, C_mid] @ [C_mid, C_in] wk_transposed = wk_reshaped.permute(0, 2, 1) # [C_out, k*k, C_mid] w_eff = torch.matmul(wk_transposed, w1) # [C_out, k*k, C_in] w_eff = w_eff.permute(0, 2, 1).view(C_out, C_in, kH, kW) # Effective bias # sum over spatial then c_mid: [C_out, C_mid] wk_sum = wk.sum(dim=[2, 3]) # [C_out, C_mid] b_eff = bk + (wk_sum * b1.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=1) # [C_out] return w_eff, b_eff ``` Wait, let me verify this math. w1[c_mid, c_in] wk[c_out, c_mid, kh, kw] effective_weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = sum_{c_mid} wk[c_out, c_mid, kh, kw] * w1[c_mid, c_in] In matrix form: For fixed c_out, kh, kw: effective[c_in] = sum_{c_mid} wk[c_mid] * w1[c_mid, c_in] = w1^T @ wk_vector (where wk_vector is [C_mid]) Or: [1, C_mid] @ [C_mid, C_in] = [1, C_in] For all kh, kw: [k*k, C_mid] @ [C_mid, C_in] = [k*k, C_in] With batch dimension c_out: [C_out, k*k, C_mid] @ [C_mid, C_in] = [C_out, k*k, C_in] In PyTorch: ```python wk_reshaped = wk.view(C_out, C_mid, kH*kW) # [C_out, C_mid, k*k] w_eff = torch.einsum('ocm,mi->oci', wk_reshaped, w1) # [C_out, k*k, C_in] w_eff = w_eff.view(C_out, kH, kW, C_in).permute(0, 3, 1, 2) # [C_out, C_in, kH, kW] ``` Wait, `einsum('ocm,mi->oci', wk_reshaped, w1)` means: - o = c_out, c = c_mid, m = k*k... wait no, I used m for spatial. Let me be more careful. wk_reshaped: [C_out, C_mid, k*k] w1: [C_mid, C_in] einsum('ocm,mi->oci'): - o = C_out, c = C_mid, m = k*k... but w1 doesn't have a dimension of size k*k. Wait, w1 shape is [C_mid, C_in]. wk_reshaped is [C_out, C_mid, k*k]. So einsum('ocm,cd->odm'): - o = c_out, c = c_mid, m = k*k - c = c_mid, d = c_in - Result: o = c_out, d = c_in, m = k*k -> [C_out, C_in, k*k] ```python w_eff = torch.einsum('ocm,cd->odm', wk_reshaped, w1) # [C_out, C_in, k*k] w_eff = w_eff.view(C_out, C_in, kH, kW) ``` Yes! This is correct and cleaner. For the bias: ```python b_eff = bk + torch.einsum('oc,cm->om', wk.view(C_out, C_mid, kH*kW).sum(dim=2), w1).squeeze(1) ``` Wait, wk.sum(dim=[2,3]) is [C_out, C_mid]. Then [C_out, C_mid] * [C_mid] (broadcasted) -> [C_out, C_mid] Sum over c_mid -> [C_out] So: b_eff = bk + (wk.sum(dim=[2,3]) * b1).sum(dim=1) Or with einsum: torch.einsum('oc,c->o', wk_sum, b1) Yes, both correct. Now let me think about whether I should implement this in Python or CUDA. Python is simpler and the precomputation is done only once in __init__. For the sequential convs in __init__: ```python class InceptionModuleNew: def __init__(self, in_channels, out_1x1, reduce_3x3, out_3x3, reduce_5x5, out_5x5, pool_proj): self.branch1x1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_1x1, kernel_size=1) self.branch3x3_1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_3x3, kernel_size=1) self.branch3x3_2 = nn.Conv2d(reduce_3x3, out_3x3, kernel_size=3, padding=1) self.branch5x5_1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_5x5, kernel_size=1) self.branch5x5_2 = nn.Conv2d(reduce_5x5, out_5x5, kernel_size=5, padding=2) self.branch_pool_conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, pool_proj, kernel_size=1) # Precompute effective weights self.branch3x3_eff_weight, self.branch3x3_eff_bias = self._compose_convs( self.branch3x3_1, self.branch3x3_2 ) self.branch5x5_eff_weight, self.branch5x5_eff_bias = self._compose_convs( self.branch5x5_1, self.branch5x5_2 ) @staticmethod def _compose_convs(conv1, conv2): w1 = conv1.weight.squeeze(-1).squeeze(-1) b1 = conv1.bias w2 = conv2.weight b2 = conv2.bias C_out, C_mid, kH, kW = w2.shape w2_reshaped = w2.view(C_out, C_mid, kH * kW) w_eff = torch.einsum('ocm,cd->odm', w2_reshaped, w1).view(C_out, w1.shape[1], kH, kW) b_eff = b2 + (w2.sum(dim=[2,3]) * b1).sum(dim=1) return w_eff, b_eff ``` Then in forward: ```python def forward(self, x, out): B, C, H, W = x.shape # branch1x1 cuda_ext.conv1x1(out[:, 0:self.out_1x1], x, self.branch1x1.weight, self.branch1x1.bias, B, C, self.out_1x1, H, W) # branch3x3 offset = self.out_1x1 cuda_ext.conv3x3(out[:, offset:offset+self.out_3x3], x, self.branch3x3_eff_weight, self.branch3x3_eff_bias, B, C, self.out_3x3, H, W) # I'll include the optimized conv1x1 kernel and simpler direct-conv kernels for 3x3/5x5, plus all the supporting infrastructure. Here's my plan for the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_fp16.h> // conv1x1 with shared memory tiling __global__ void conv1x1_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE * (C_IN_TILE + 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; int c_in_per_thread = C_IN_TILE / C_OUT_TILE; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load input for (int i = 0; i < c_in_per_thread; i++) { int c_in_local = local_c_out * c_in_per_thread + i; int global_c_in = c_in_base + c_in_local; if (global_c_in < C_in) { int in_addr = ((b * C_in + global_c_in) * H + h) * W + w; s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; } } // Load weights int tid = local_hw + local_c_out * blockDim.x; int w_load_idx = tid; if (w_load_idx < C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE) { int w_c_out = w_load_idx / C_IN_TILE; int w_c_in = w_load_idx % C_IN_TILE; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; int global_w_c_in = c_in_base + w_c_in; if (global_w_c_out < C_out && global_w_c_in < C_in) { s_weight[w_load_idx] = weight[global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in]; } else { s_weight[w_load_idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE; c_in_local++) { sum += s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]; } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = sum; } __global__ void conv1x1_relu_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { const int SPATIAL_TILE = 32; const int C_OUT_TILE = 8; const int C_IN_TILE = 32; int hw = H * W; int b = blockIdx.z; int c_out_block = blockIdx.x * C_OUT_TILE; int hw_block = blockIdx.y * SPATIAL_TILE; int local_hw = threadIdx.x; int local_c_out = threadIdx.y; int global_hw = hw_block + local_hw; int global_c_out = c_out_block + local_c_out; if (global_hw >= hw || global_c_out >= C_out) return; int h = global_hw / W; int w = global_hw % W; __shared__ float s_in[SPATIAL_TILE * (C_IN_TILE + 1)]; __shared__ float s_weight[C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE]; float sum = bias[global_c_out]; int c_in_per_thread = C_IN_TILE / C_OUT_TILE; for (int c_in_base = 0; c_in_base < C_in; c_in_base += C_IN_TILE) { // Load input for (int i = 0; i < c_in_per_thread; i++) { int c_in_local = local_c_out * c_in_per_thread + i; int global_c_in = c_in_base + c_in_local; if (global_c_in < C_in) { int in_addr = ((b * C_in + global_c_in) * H + h) * W + w; s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] = in[in_addr]; } } // Load weights int tid = local_hw + local_c_out * blockDim.x; int w_load_idx = tid; if (w_load_idx < C_OUT_TILE * C_IN_TILE) { int w_c_out = w_load_idx / C_IN_TILE; int w_c_in = w_load_idx % C_IN_TILE; int global_w_c_out = c_out_block + w_c_out; int global_w_c_in = c_in_base + w_c_in; if (global_w_c_out < C_out && global_w_c_in < C_in) { s_weight[w_load_idx] = weight[global_w_c_out * C_in + global_w_c_in]; } else { s_weight[w_load_idx] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); #pragma unroll for (int c_in_local = 0; c_in_local < C_IN_TILE; c_in_local++) { sum += s_in[local_hw * (C_IN_TILE + 1) + c_in_local] * s_weight[local_c_out * C_IN_TILE + c_in_local]; } __syncthreads(); } int out_addr = ((b * C_out + global_c_out) * H + h) * W + w; out[out_addr] = fmaxf(sum, 0.0f); } // conv3x3 - simple direct conv __global__ void conv3x3_kernel( float* __restrict__ out, const float* __restrict__ in, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int hw = H * W; int total = B * C_out * hw; int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W; int tmp = idx / W; int h = tmp % H; tmp = tmp / H; int c_out = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; float sum = bias[c_out]; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { #pragma unroll for (int dy = -1; dy <= 1; dy++) { int in_h = h + dy; if (in_h < 0 || in_h >= H) continue; #pragma unroll for (int dx = -1; dx <= 1; dx++) { int in_w = w + dx; if (in_w < 0 || in_w >= W) continue; int in_idx = ((b * C_in + c_in) * H + in_h) * W + in_w; int w_idx = ((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + (dy+1)) * 3 + (dx+1); sum += in[in_idx] * weight[w_idx]; } } } out[idx] = sum; } __global__ void conv3x3_relu_kernel(...) { ... with fmaxf(sum, 0.0f) ... } // conv5x5 - simple direct conv __global__ void conv5x5_kernel(...) { similar, dy,dx from -2 to 2 } // conv7x7 stride 2 __global__ void conv7x7_stride2_relu_kernel(...) { ... } // maxpool3x3 stride 2 __global__ void maxpool3x3_stride2_kernel(...) { ... } // maxpool3x3 stride 1 + 1x1 conv __global__ void maxpool3x3_1x1_kernel(...) { ... } // global avgpool __global__ void global_avgpool_kernel(...) { ... } // fc __global__ void fc_kernel(...) { ... } // C++ wrappers void conv1x1_cuda(torch::Tensor out, torch::Tensor in, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W) { dim3 block(32, 8); dim3 grid((C_out + 7) / 8, (H * W + 31) / 32, B); conv1x1_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(out.data_ptr<float>(), in.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, C_in, C_out, H, W); } // ... all other wrappers ... """ # Python part class InceptionModuleNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_1x1, reduce_3x3, out_3x3, reduce_5x5, out_5x5, pool_proj): super().__init__() self.out_1x1 = out_1x1 self.out_3x3 = out_3x3 self.out_5x5 = out_5x5 self.pool_proj = pool_proj self.branch1x1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_1x1, kernel_size=1) self.branch3x3_1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_3x3, kernel_size=1) self.branch3x3_2 = nn.Conv2d(reduce_3x3, out_3x3, kernel_size=3, padding=1) self.branch5x5_1 = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, reduce_5x5, kernel_size=1) self.branch5x5_2 = nn.Conv2d(reduce_5x5, out_5x5, kernel_size=5, padding=2) self.branch_pool_conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, pool_proj, kernel_size=1) # The `_init_weights` approach means the effective weights are computed after initialization. But PyTorch might move the model to CUDA after creation, so the buffers need to be on the correct device. A better approach: compute effective weights lazily in the first forward pass, or in `forward` itself, caching the result. But since weights don't change (we're in eval mode typically), computing once is fine. Actually, I can create the InceptionModuleNew normally, and since register_buffer is used, the buffers will be moved to CUDA when the whole model is moved to CUDA. But I need to populate them first. I can do it in InceptionModuleNew.__init__ right after creating the conv layers. ```python def __init__(self, ...): # ... create layers ... with torch.no_grad(): # compute eff weights ... self.register_buffer('branch3x3_w_eff', w_eff) self.register_buffer('branch3x3_b_eff', b_eff) ``` This should work because at `__init__` time, the conv layers are created on CPU (or wherever the module is being created). When we move ModelNew to CUDA with `.cuda()`, these buffers will also be moved. Actually wait, `.cuda()` is called on the model after creation. The weights will be on CPU during __init__ and moved to CUDA later. The `register_buffer` call registers the tensors, and they'll follow the module's device. For `load_inline`, the compiled extension is loaded at module load time, and it handles CUDA tensors passed to it. assistantThe previous run failed correctness because of two bugs: (1) `torch.cat` views passed to CUDA kernels aren’t contiguous, so linear indexing inside the kernels writes to the wrong memory locations; and (2) the shared-memory conv1x1 kernel leaves uninitialized shared-memory cells when `C_in` isn’t a multiple of 32, causing NaNs. I’ll fix this by: 1. **Using simple 1-D-grid direct kernels for every op** — no shared memory, no templates, just straightforward element-wise loops. This guarantees correctness for arbitrary sizes. 2. **Building each Inception branch into its own contiguous tensor and `torch.cat` them together** in Python. This avoids any slicing stride issues.

callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\ncuda_source = r\"\"\"\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n\n// ---- conv1x1 (no relu) ----\n__global__ void conv1x1_kernel(\n float* __restrict__ out,\n const float* __restrict__ in_data,\n const float* __restrict__ weight,\n const float* __restrict__ bias,\n int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W\n) {\n int hw = H * W;\n int total = B * C_out * hw;\n int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x +
